BiologyLessons — Unit6

--- Lesson 6.1: Introduction to Cells (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Introduction to Cells
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is an atom, and what are its three fundamental subatomic particles?
The atom concept evolved from Dalton's atomic theory (1803) through Thomson's discovery of electrons (1897, 'plum pudding' model), Rutherford's nuclear model (1911, gold foil experiment), and Bohr's orbital model (1913). Modern quantum mechanical models describe electron probability clouds (orbitals). Protons have a mass of ~1 amu and charge of +1; neutrons have ~1 amu and zero charge; electrons have ~1/1836 amu and charge of -1. In a neutral atom, protons = electrons. The nucleus contains >99.9% of the atom's mass despite being ~100,000× smaller than the overall atom.
What is an element, and which elements are most important in biological systems?
Of the 118 known elements (organized in Mendeleev's periodic table, 1869), only about 25 are essential for life. The 'big four' (CHON) make up ~96% of body mass: carbon forms the backbone of organic molecules, hydrogen is in virtually all biological molecules, oxygen is critical for aerobic respiration, and nitrogen is in all amino acids and nucleic acids. Additional important elements include phosphorus (P, in ATP and DNA), sulfur (S, in certain amino acids), calcium (Ca, bones and signaling), potassium (K, nerve impulses), and sodium (Na, fluid balance). Trace elements (iron in hemoglobin, zinc in 300+ enzymes, iodine in thyroid hormones) are needed in tiny amounts but are vital.
What is a covalent bond, and how do polar and nonpolar covalent bonds differ?
Covalent bonds involve electron sharing and are the most common bonds in biological molecules. Linus Pauling's electronegativity scale (1932) quantifies the tendency of atoms to attract shared electrons: F (4.0) > O (3.44) > N (3.04) > C (2.55) > H (2.20). When electronegativity difference is <0.5, the bond is nonpolar covalent (e.g., C-H, ~0.35 difference). When 0.5-1.7, it's polar covalent (e.g., O-H, 1.24 difference, creating partial charges δ+ on H and δ- on O). Above ~1.7, bonds are ionic. Water's polarity from its polar O-H bonds and bent shape (104.5°) makes it life's universal solvent — arguably the most biologically important chemical property on Earth.
What is an ionic bond, and how does it differ from a covalent bond?
Ionic bonds form when the electronegativity difference between atoms exceeds ~1.7 (e.g., Na 0.93 and Cl 3.16, difference = 2.23). The metal atom loses electron(s) to form a cation (positive ion) and the nonmetal gains them to form an anion (negative ion). Ionic compounds dissociate in water to form electrolytes — critical in biology: Na⁺/K⁺ maintain nerve impulse conduction (Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase pump), Ca²⁺ triggers muscle contraction and neurotransmitter release, Cl⁻ is involved in gastric acid (HCl) production, and Fe²⁺/Fe³⁺ bind oxygen in hemoglobin. Electrolyte imbalances can be life-threatening (cardiac arrhythmias from potassium imbalance).
Outliers and Resistant Measures
Ocean acidification integrates several chemistry concepts: (1) CO₂ dissolution and acid formation: CO₂ + H₂O → H₂CO₃ (carbonic acid). (2) Dissociation: H₂CO₃ → H⁺ + HCO₃⁻ → 2H⁺ + CO₃²⁻. (3) pH logarithmic scale: 0.1 decrease = 26% more H⁺ (10^0.1 ≈ 1.26). (4) Ionic equilibrium: excess H⁺ combines with CO₃²⁻, reducing carbonate availability. (5) Impact on biomineralization: organisms like corals (aragonite), oysters, and pteropods need sufficient carbonate saturation (Ω > 1) to build CaCO₃ shells. Below Ω = 1, shells dissolve. Projections: pH may reach 7.7-7.8 by 2100 (RCP8.5), a 150% H⁺ increase from pre-industrial levels. Pteropod shell dissolution is already observed in the Southern Ocean.
Real-World Applications
Example: Carbon-14 (¹⁴C) is a radioactive isotope with a half-life of 5,730 years that is continuously produced in the atmosphere by cosmic ray bombardment of nitrogen-14. Living organisms maintain the same ¹⁴C/¹²C ratio as the atmosphere through constant carbon exchange, but this ratio starts declining at death as ¹⁴C decays. In 1991, hikers in the Alps discovered 'Ötzi the Iceman' — the mummified body of a Copper Age man. Radiocarbon dating determined he died ~5,300 years ago. Explain how isotopes make this dating possible.
Radiocarbon dating (developed by Willard Libby, 1949 — won Nobel Prize 1960) exploits the radioactive decay of ¹⁴C: (1) Cosmic rays create ¹⁴C in the atmosphere: ¹⁴N + neutron → ¹⁴C + proton. (2) ¹⁴C is incorporated into CO₂, then into organisms through photosynthesis and food chains. (3) At death, ¹⁴C intake stops; existing ¹⁴C decays via beta emission: ¹⁴C → ¹⁴N + β⁻. (4) Half-life = 5,730 years, so after 1 half-life, 50% remains; after 2, 25%; etc. The method is reliable to ~50,000 years (~9 half-lives). For Ötzi (died ~3300 BCE), roughly 60% of original ¹⁴C remained. Modern AMS (accelerator mass spectrometry) requires only milligram samples. Other radioisotopes date different timescales: K-40/Ar-40 for millions of years (used to date hominin fossils).
Example: Geckos can climb vertical glass surfaces and even hang upside down from ceilings, supporting their entire body weight using Van der Waals forces alone. Each gecko foot has ~500,000 setae (hair-like structures), each splitting into 100-1,000 spatulae (~200 nm tips) that maximize molecular contact with surfaces. This has inspired biomimetic adhesive materials in engineering. Evaluate how this example connects fundamental intermolecular forces to macroscopic biological function and technology.
Gecko adhesion (Autumn et al., 2000, Nature) demonstrates Van der Waals forces at the biological scale: (1) Physical mechanism — spatulae tips (<200 nm) conform to surface irregularities at the nanometer scale, maximizing intermolecular contact area. (2) Force generation — Van der Waals attraction (London dispersion forces from temporary dipoles) at each contact point is tiny (~10 nN per spatula), but ~2 billion spatulae generate ~20-40N total force. (3) Easy detachment — geckos peel toes at a 30° angle, reducing contact area and adhesion instantly. (4) Self-cleaning — the sticky system doesn't accumulate dirt because Van der Waals forces between dirt and the surface exceed those between dirt and spatulae. (5) Biomimicry applications: Geckskin (holds 700 lbs on glass), surgical adhesive (wet tissue bonding), and robotic grippers (NASA's LEMUR robot for spacecraft inspection).
Example: Heavy metals like lead (Pb) and mercury (Hg) are toxic even in trace amounts because they disrupt protein structure and enzyme function. Lead exposure (from old paint, contaminated water — as in the Flint, Michigan water crisis of 2014) causes neurological damage especially in children. Mercury (from contaminated fish — as in Minamata disease, Japan, 1956) causes severe nervous system damage. Analyze how these metals interfere with chemical bonding in biological systems.
Heavy metal toxicity illustrates chemical bonding principles: (1) Sulfhydryl targeting — Pb²⁺ and Hg²⁺ have very high affinity for -SH groups (cysteine), forming stable metal-sulfur bonds that are stronger than normal S-S disulfide bridges, permanently disrupting protein folding. (2) Molecular mimicry — Pb²⁺ mimics Ca²⁺ (similar ionic radius), entering neurons through calcium channels, disrupting neurotransmitter release and synaptic function (explaining children's developmental neurotoxicity). (3) Enzyme inhibition — lead inhibits δ-aminolevulinic acid dehydratase (ALAD) in heme synthesis, causing anemia. (4) Bioaccumulation — methylmercury (CH₃Hg⁺) biomagnifies through food chains (Minamata: factory discharged mercury → methylated by bacteria → fish → humans). (5) Chelation therapy (EDTA, DMSA) works by providing multiple electron-pair donor groups that form stronger coordination bonds with the metal than the biological targets, literally pulling metals away from proteins.

--- Lesson 6.1: Introduction to Cells (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What is an atom, and what are its three fundamental subatomic particles?
    [ ] A theoretical construct used only in physics with no relevance to biological systems
    [*] The smallest unit of an element that retains its chemical properties, composed of protons (positive charge, in nucleus), neutrons (no charge, in nucleus), and electrons (negative charge, in orbitals surrounding the nucleus)
    [ ] The smallest unit of a compound, made of cells, organelles, and membranes
    [ ] A microscopic particle visible only under electron microscopes, consisting of quarks and gluons
    Explanation: The atom concept evolved from Dalton's atomic theory (1803) through Thomson's discovery of electrons (1897, 'plum pudding' model), Rutherford's nuclear model (1911, gold foil experiment), and Bohr's orbital model (1913). Modern quantum mechanical models describe electron probability clouds (orbitals). Protons have a mass of ~1 amu and charge of +1; neutrons have ~1 amu and zero charge; electrons have ~1/1836 amu and charge of -1. In a neutral atom, protons = electrons. The nucleus contains >99.9% of the atom's mass despite being ~100,000× smaller than the overall atom.

  Q2. What is an element, and which elements are most important in biological systems?
    [ ] A chemical compound containing two or more different types of atoms bonded in specific proportions
    [ ] Any naturally occurring substance found on the periodic table that can exist in solid, liquid, or gaseous states
    [ ] A mixture of different atoms combined in fixed ratios, with iron (Fe) and gold (Au) being the most biologically important
    [*] A pure substance made of only one type of atom, identified by its atomic number — with carbon (C), hydrogen (H), oxygen (O), and nitrogen (N) making up approximately 96% of the human body's mass, along with essential trace elements like iron, zinc, and iodine
    Explanation: Of the 118 known elements (organized in Mendeleev's periodic table, 1869), only about 25 are essential for life. The 'big four' (CHON) make up ~96% of body mass: carbon forms the backbone of organic molecules, hydrogen is in virtually all biological molecules, oxygen is critical for aerobic respiration, and nitrogen is in all amino acids and nucleic acids. Additional important elements include phosphorus (P, in ATP and DNA), sulfur (S, in certain amino acids), calcium (Ca, bones and signaling), potassium (K, nerve impulses), and sodium (Na, fluid balance). Trace elements (iron in hemoglobin, zinc in 300+ enzymes, iodine in thyroid hormones) are needed in tiny amounts but are vital.

  Q3. What determines an atom's identity, and how do isotopes differ from each other?
    [ ] All atoms of the same element are perfectly identical with no natural variation in subatomic particle composition
    [ ] An atom's identity is determined by its number of electrons, and isotopes differ in the number of protons
    [ ] An atom's identity is determined by its mass number, and isotopes have different numbers of electrons while keeping the same protons and neutrons
    [*] An atom's identity (which element it is) is determined by its atomic number — the number of protons in its nucleus — while isotopes are atoms of the same element with the same number of protons but different numbers of neutrons, giving them different mass numbers
    Explanation: The atomic number (Z) = number of protons and uniquely identifies each element (e.g., carbon always has 6 protons). The mass number (A) = protons + neutrons. Isotopes have the same Z but different A: carbon-12 (6p + 6n, 98.9%), carbon-13 (6p + 7n, 1.1%), and carbon-14 (6p + 8n, trace — radioactive). Radioactive isotopes (radioisotopes) are biologically important: carbon-14 dating estimates ages of organic materials up to ~50,000 years; iodine-131 treats thyroid cancer; fluorine-18 is used in PET scans; phosphorus-32 traces DNA and RNA in experiments. The atomic mass listed on the periodic table is the weighted average of all naturally occurring isotopes.

  Q4. What are electron shells and valence electrons, and why do they matter for chemical bonding?
    [ ] Electron shells are protective barriers around atoms that prevent bonding, and valence electrons are electrons trapped inside the nucleus
    [ ] Electron shells contain equal numbers of protons and electrons, and valence electrons refer to any electron that has been removed from the atom
    [ ] Electron shells are relevant only to metals and have no role in the bonding of biological molecules like carbon and oxygen
    [*] Electron shells are energy levels surrounding the nucleus where electrons reside (first shell holds 2, second holds 8, third holds 18), and valence electrons — those in the outermost shell — determine an atom's chemical bonding behavior, following the octet rule (atoms tend to bond until they have 8 valence electrons for stability)
    Explanation: Electron configuration determines bonding: the first shell (n=1) holds max 2 electrons (1s²), the second (n=2) holds max 8 (2s²2p⁶), the third (n=3) holds max 18. Valence electrons are in the outermost occupied shell. Carbon has 4 valence electrons (2,4 configuration) and needs 4 more to satisfy the octet rule — explaining why carbon forms 4 covalent bonds. Oxygen has 6 valence electrons and needs 2 more. Hydrogen is an exception — it follows the 'duet rule' (needs only 2 electrons). Noble gases (He, Ne, Ar) have full outer shells and are chemically inert. Understanding electron configuration explains why water is H₂O (not H₃O), why CO₂ has double bonds, and why methane is CH₄.

  Q5. What is a covalent bond, and how do polar and nonpolar covalent bonds differ?
    [*] A bond formed when atoms share electron pairs — nonpolar covalent bonds share electrons equally (e.g., O₂, N₂) while polar covalent bonds share electrons unequally due to differences in electronegativity (e.g., water's O-H bonds, where oxygen pulls electrons more strongly than hydrogen)
    [ ] A type of bond that only exists between carbon atoms in organic molecules and cannot form between other elements
    [ ] A bond formed by transferring electrons from one atom to another, where polar means the bond involves metals and nonpolar involves nonmetals
    [ ] A weak intermolecular force that holds molecules near each other without directly linking atoms together
    Explanation: Covalent bonds involve electron sharing and are the most common bonds in biological molecules. Linus Pauling's electronegativity scale (1932) quantifies the tendency of atoms to attract shared electrons: F (4.0) > O (3.44) > N (3.04) > C (2.55) > H (2.20). When electronegativity difference is <0.5, the bond is nonpolar covalent (e.g., C-H, ~0.35 difference). When 0.5-1.7, it's polar covalent (e.g., O-H, 1.24 difference, creating partial charges δ+ on H and δ- on O). Above ~1.7, bonds are ionic. Water's polarity from its polar O-H bonds and bent shape (104.5°) makes it life's universal solvent — arguably the most biologically important chemical property on Earth.

  Q6. What is an ionic bond, and how does it differ from a covalent bond?
    [*] An ionic bond forms when one atom transfers electrons to another, creating oppositely charged ions (cation + and anion -) that attract each other — unlike covalent bonds where electrons are shared; examples include NaCl (table salt) where Na loses an electron to become Na⁺ and Cl gains it to become Cl⁻
    [ ] Ionic bonds only form between hydrogen and oxygen atoms, while covalent bonds form between all other elements
    [ ] An ionic bond involves sharing electrons equally between two nonmetal atoms, while covalent bonds involve magnetic attraction
    [ ] Ionic bonds and covalent bonds are the same type of interaction, just named differently depending on the textbook being used
    Explanation: Ionic bonds form when the electronegativity difference between atoms exceeds ~1.7 (e.g., Na 0.93 and Cl 3.16, difference = 2.23). The metal atom loses electron(s) to form a cation (positive ion) and the nonmetal gains them to form an anion (negative ion). Ionic compounds dissociate in water to form electrolytes — critical in biology: Na⁺/K⁺ maintain nerve impulse conduction (Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase pump), Ca²⁺ triggers muscle contraction and neurotransmitter release, Cl⁻ is involved in gastric acid (HCl) production, and Fe²⁺/Fe³⁺ bind oxygen in hemoglobin. Electrolyte imbalances can be life-threatening (cardiac arrhythmias from potassium imbalance).

  Q7. What are hydrogen bonds, and why are they critically important in biology?
    [ ] Strong covalent bonds between hydrogen atoms that hold molecules together permanently and cannot be broken without extreme force
    [ ] The same thing as ionic bonds, since hydrogen always completely transfers its electron to other atoms
    [ ] Bonds that only exist between two hydrogen atoms in a hydrogen gas molecule (H₂)
    [*] Weak electrostatic attractions between a partially positive hydrogen atom (bonded to an electronegative atom like O or N) and a nearby electronegative atom — individually weak but collectively essential for water's properties, DNA's double helix structure, and protein folding
    Explanation: Hydrogen bonds (~5% the strength of covalent bonds, ~20 kJ/mol vs ~400 kJ/mol) form between a hydrogen atom covalently bonded to an electronegative atom (O, N, or F) and another electronegative atom. Their biological importance is immense: (1) Water properties — cohesion, surface tension, high specific heat (4.18 J/g·°C), high heat of vaporization (2,260 J/g), and ice floating (anomalous expansion). (2) DNA — A-T base pairs have 2 H-bonds, G-C pairs have 3, stabilizing the double helix while allowing strand separation during replication. (3) Protein structure — H-bonds create alpha-helices and beta-sheets in secondary structure. Each water molecule can form up to 4 hydrogen bonds with neighboring molecules.

  Q8. Why is carbon the central element of organic chemistry and the basis of all known life?
    [ ] Carbon is the most abundant element on Earth, making it readily available for biological reactions
    [ ] Carbon atoms are living units and each one contains miniature cells that replicate independently
    [ ] Carbon is the only element that can form covalent bonds, so life had no other chemical option to build molecules around
    [*] Carbon has 4 valence electrons allowing it to form 4 stable covalent bonds with diverse atoms (including other carbons), creating the backbone of virtually unlimited molecular diversity — from simple methane (CH₄) to complex proteins with thousands of atoms, including chains, rings, and branched structures
    Explanation: Carbon's tetravalence (4 valence electrons, electron configuration 1s²2s²2p²) allows it to: (1) Form single, double, or triple bonds (C-C, C=C, C≡C), (2) create long chains, branched structures, and rings (benzene, cyclohexane, glucose), (3) bond with many different elements (H, O, N, P, S), and (4) create isomers (molecules with same formula but different structures — glucose and fructose are both C₆H₁₂O₆). Carbon-carbon bonds are strong (~346 kJ/mol for single bonds) yet flexible enough to create functional groups: hydroxyl (-OH), amino (-NH₂), carboxyl (-COOH), phosphate (-PO₄), sulfhydryl (-SH), and carbonyl (C=O). These functional groups determine the properties of the four major macromolecules: carbohydrates, lipids, proteins, and nucleic acids.

  Q9. What does the pH scale measure, and why is pH regulation critical for living organisms?
    [ ] pH measures the concentration of oxygen dissolved in a solution, with higher values indicating more oxygen available for respiration
    [ ] pH measures the temperature of a solution, and organisms only survive at precisely neutral pH 7.0
    [ ] pH is a measure of an element's position on the periodic table, with acids found on the left and bases on the right
    [*] pH measures the concentration of hydrogen ions (H⁺) in a solution on a logarithmic scale from 0 (most acidic) to 14 (most basic), with 7 being neutral — biological systems require precise pH control because even small changes can denature enzymes and disrupt biochemical reactions (e.g., human blood pH must stay between 7.35-7.45)
    Explanation: The pH scale (developed by Sørensen, 1909) is logarithmic: pH = -log[H⁺]. Each unit represents a 10-fold change in H⁺ concentration — so pH 5 is 10× more acidic than pH 6 and 100× more than pH 7. Biological pH ranges vary dramatically: stomach acid pH ~1.5-3.5 (HCl from parietal cells), blood pH 7.35-7.45 (tightly regulated), lysosome pH ~4.5-5.0 (for enzyme activity), small intestine pH ~7.5-8.0. Blood pH outside 6.8-7.8 is lethal because enzymes have optimal pH ranges — pepsin works best at pH 2 but trypsin at pH 8. Buffer systems (bicarbonate, phosphate, and protein buffers) resist pH changes and are essential for homeostasis.

  Q10. What are Van der Waals forces, and how do they contribute to biological systems?
    [*] Weak, short-range intermolecular attractions arising from temporary fluctuations in electron distribution (London dispersion forces) — individually very weak but collectively significant in protein folding, enzyme-substrate binding, and even gecko adhesion (billions of setae utilizing Van der Waals forces to support the gecko's body weight)
    [ ] Strong intramolecular bonds that hold atoms together within a single molecule, similar to covalent bonds in strength based on established principles
    [ ] A type of hydrogen bond that only occurs between very large macromolecules like DNA and RNA, particularly regarding whose in the context of this analysis
    [ ] Forces of repulsion that keep molecules apart, explaining why substances maintain distinct phases, particularly regarding that based on the prevailing evidence
    Explanation: Van der Waals forces (including London dispersion forces and dipole-dipole interactions) are the weakest intermolecular interactions (~0.4-4 kJ/mol) but are ubiquitous and biologically significant. In proteins, Van der Waals interactions between hydrophobic amino acid side chains contribute to tertiary structure folding. In enzyme-substrate binding, the complementary shapes allow hundreds of Van der Waals contacts that collectively provide significant binding energy. The remarkable example of gecko adhesion demonstrates their power: each gecko foot has ~500,000 setae, each splitting into hundreds of spatulae, creating billions of contact points whose cumulative Van der Waals forces can support ~20× the gecko's body weight — inspiring biomimetic adhesive materials.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes 'atom'?
    [*] The smallest unit of an element that retains the chemical properties of that element, consisting of a nucleus (protons and neutrons) surrounded by electrons in energy levels — the fundamental building block from which all matter is constructed
    [ ] A microscopic cell fragment that functions as the basic unit of living organisms
    [ ] A cluster of identical molecules held together by covalent bonds forming a visible crystal structure
    [ ] The smallest unit of a molecule that can exist independently in a solution
    Explanation: An atom (from Greek 'atomos' meaning 'indivisible') is the fundamental unit of matter. While atoms were once thought indivisible, we now know they contain subatomic particles: protons (charge +1, mass ~1 amu), neutrons (charge 0, mass ~1 amu), and electrons (charge -1, mass ~1/1836 amu). The number of protons defines the element (atomic number). Atoms range from hydrogen (1 proton) to oganesson (118 protons). In biology, atoms combine to form molecules (H₂O, CO₂, C₆H₁₂O₆) that compose cells and organisms.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes 'element'?
    [ ] Any substance that contains more than one type of atom bonded in fixed ratios
    [*] A pure substance consisting of only one type of atom, which cannot be broken down into simpler substances by ordinary chemical means — identified by its unique atomic number, with 118 known elements organized in the periodic table
    [ ] A mixture of two or more atoms that interact without forming chemical bonds
    [ ] A component of the periodic table that only exists in laboratory conditions and has no natural occurrence
    Explanation: Elements are pure substances defined by their atomic number (number of protons). The periodic table organizes 118 elements by atomic number and chemical properties. Of these, about 25 are essential for life: carbon (C), hydrogen (H), oxygen (O), nitrogen (N) constitute ~96% of body mass; phosphorus (P), sulfur (S), calcium (Ca), potassium (K), sodium (Na) are major minerals; and trace elements (iron, zinc, copper, iodine, selenium) are needed in small amounts for specific functions.

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes 'covalent bond'?
    [ ] A weak attraction between molecules caused by temporary fluctuations in electron clouds
    [ ] A metallic bond where electrons flow freely in a 'sea of electrons' shared among many metal atoms
    [ ] A bond formed when one atom completely transfers its electrons to another atom, creating charged ions
    [*] A chemical bond formed when two atoms share one or more pairs of electrons, allowing both atoms to achieve stable electron configurations — the predominant bond type in biological molecules, with polar covalent bonds (unequal sharing) and nonpolar covalent bonds (equal sharing)
    Explanation: Covalent bonds are the most common bonds in organic molecules. Single bonds share 1 electron pair (C-C, 346 kJ/mol), double bonds share 2 pairs (C=O, 745 kJ/mol), and triple bonds share 3 pairs (N≡N, 945 kJ/mol). Polar covalent bonds (O-H, N-H) create partial charges essential for hydrogen bonding and water's solvent properties. Virtually all biological macromolecules — carbohydrates, lipids, proteins, nucleic acids — are held together primarily by covalent bonds.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes 'ionic bond'?
    [*] A chemical bond formed by the complete transfer of one or more electrons from one atom to another, creating oppositely charged ions (cation⁺ and anion⁻) that are held together by electrostatic attraction — these bonds typically form between metals and nonmetals and dissociate in water to form electrolytes
    [ ] A bond that exists only within the nucleus of an atom, holding protons and neutrons together within the typical range of values
    [ ] A bond where two atoms share electrons equally in a symmetrical arrangement between their nuclei based on established principles
    [ ] A temporary attraction between nonpolar molecules caused by induced dipole interactions, particularly regarding difference as observed in standard experiments
    Explanation: Ionic bonds form when the electronegativity difference exceeds ~1.7. In NaCl, sodium (group 1) loses its single valence electron to become Na⁺, while chlorine (group 17) gains it to become Cl⁻. In water, ionic compounds dissociate into ions (electrolytes) essential for biology: Na⁺/K⁺ (nerve impulses, maintained by Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase), Ca²⁺ (muscle contraction, bone structure), Fe²⁺/Fe³⁺ (hemoglobin, cytochromes), and Cl⁻ (stomach acid). Electrolyte imbalances cause serious conditions: hypokalemia (low K⁺) causes cardiac arrhythmias, hypocalcemia (low Ca²⁺) causes tetany.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes 'hydrogen bond'?
    [ ] A strong intramolecular bond that permanently links hydrogen to oxygen in every water molecule, particularly regarding specific as determined by standard methodology
    [ ] An bond that only forms between hydrogen atoms and carbon atoms in organic molecules, particularly regarding each in most standard conditions
    [ ] A covalent bond between two hydrogen atoms (H-H) in a hydrogen gas molecule, particularly regarding creating within the typical range of values
    [*] A relatively weak electrostatic attraction (~5% the strength of a covalent bond) between a partially positive hydrogen atom covalently bonded to an electronegative atom (O, N, or F) and a nearby electronegative atom — individually weak but collectively essential for water's unique properties, DNA base pairing, and protein secondary structure
    Explanation: Hydrogen bonds (~20 kJ/mol, compared to ~400 kJ/mol for covalent bonds) arise from the attraction between δ+ hydrogen and δ- electronegative atoms. In water, each molecule can form 4 H-bonds, creating a dynamic network responsible for: high specific heat (moderating body temperature), high heat of vaporization (evaporative cooling), cohesion (xylem water transport), and ice's lower density (ice floats, insulating aquatic habitats). In DNA, A-T pairs form 2 H-bonds and G-C pairs form 3, explaining why G-C-rich regions require higher melting temperatures. In proteins, H-bonds create alpha-helices and beta-sheets.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes 'isotope'?
    [ ] Atoms that have been artificially created in particle accelerators and do not occur naturally
    [*] Atoms of the same element (same number of protons) that have different numbers of neutrons, resulting in different mass numbers — some isotopes are stable while others are radioactive (radioisotopes), which are used in biological research and medicine
    [ ] An atom that has gained or lost electrons, giving it a net electrical charge
    [ ] Different elements that happen to have the same mass number but different atomic numbers
    Explanation: Isotopes share the same atomic number (protons) but differ in mass number (protons + neutrons). Carbon has three natural isotopes: C-12 (98.9%, stable), C-13 (1.1%, stable, used in NMR), C-14 (trace, radioactive, half-life 5,730 years — used in radiocarbon dating). Radioisotopes are invaluable in biology/medicine: I-131 (thyroid cancer treatment), F-18 (PET scans for cancer detection), P-32 (tracing DNA in Hershey-Chase experiment, 1952), and Tc-99m (most common medical imaging isotope).

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes 'electronegativity'?
    [ ] The energy required to remove an electron from an atom in its gaseous state, which decreases down the periodic table
    [ ] The negative charge carried by electrons that balances the positive charge of protons in a neutral atom
    [*] The tendency of an atom to attract shared electrons in a chemical bond — quantified by Pauling's scale where fluorine (4.0) is most electronegative and electronegativity differences between bonded atoms determine whether bonds are nonpolar covalent, polar covalent, or ionic
    [ ] The repulsive force between electron clouds that prevents atoms from merging together
    Explanation: Electronegativity (Pauling scale, 1932) determines bond type: equal electronegativity → nonpolar covalent (C-C, O=O); moderate difference (0.5-1.7) → polar covalent (O-H, N-H); large difference (>1.7) → ionic (Na-Cl). Key biological values: O (3.44), N (3.04), C (2.55), H (2.20). Water's polarity arises from O-H electronegativity difference (1.24): oxygen pulls electrons more strongly, creating partial charges (δ- on O, δ+ on H). This polarity makes water an excellent solvent for ionic and polar substances — critical for virtually all biological chemistry.

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes 'ion'?
    [*] An atom or molecule that has gained or lost one or more electrons, giving it a net positive charge (cation, lost electrons) or net negative charge (anion, gained electrons) — ions are essential in biology as electrolytes for nerve signaling, muscle contraction, and maintaining osmotic balance
    [ ] A molecule that can act as both an acid and a base depending on the pH of the solution, particularly regarding determining as typically measured in practice
    [ ] An isotope of an atom that has a different number of neutrons than the most common form, particularly regarding contraction in most standard conditions
    [ ] A neutral atom that exists in an excited state with electrons temporarily in higher energy levels when evaluated systematically
    Explanation: Ions form when atoms gain or lose electrons: Na → Na⁺ + e⁻ (cation), Cl + e⁻ → Cl⁻ (anion). Biologically critical ions include: Na⁺ and K⁺ (nerve impulse conduction via Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase, maintaining resting membrane potential of -70mV), Ca²⁺ (muscle contraction trigger, bone structure, neurotransmitter release, second messenger), Cl⁻ (gastric acid production, GABA receptor function), Fe²⁺/Fe³⁺ (oxygen transport in hemoglobin), Mg²⁺ (cofactor for 300+ enzymes, chlorophyll center), and H⁺/OH⁻ (determining pH). Electrolyte balance is maintained by kidneys and is critical for survival.

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes 'pH'?
    [ ] The ratio of protons to hydrogen atoms in any chemical compound containing the element hydrogen
    [ ] A linear scale from 1 to 10 measuring the proportion of hydrogen gas dissolved in a liquid solution
    [ ] A measurement of the physical hardness of water based on its dissolved mineral content
    [*] A logarithmic scale from 0 to 14 measuring the concentration of hydrogen ions (H⁺) in a solution — where pH 7 is neutral, values below 7 are acidic (more H⁺), and values above 7 are basic (fewer H⁺), with each whole number representing a 10-fold change in H⁺ concentration
    Explanation: pH (from German 'potenz des Wasserstoffs' — power of hydrogen, Sørensen 1909) = -log₁₀[H⁺]. Key biological pH values: gastric juice (1.5-3.5), lysosomes (4.5-5.0), cytoplasm (~7.2), blood (7.35-7.45), pancreatic secretion (~8.0). The logarithmic nature means pH 1 has 10× more H⁺ than pH 2 and 1,000,000× more than pH 7. Enzymes function within narrow pH ranges (pepsin optimal pH ~2, trypsin ~8, catalase ~7). Buffer systems (bicarbonate, phosphate, protein) resist pH changes and are essential for homeostasis.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes 'buffer'?
    [*] A chemical system (usually a weak acid and its conjugate base) that resists changes in pH when small amounts of acid or base are added — essential in biological systems where even slight pH changes can denature proteins and disrupt enzyme function
    [ ] A strong acid that completely dissociates in water to release all its hydrogen ions at once
    [ ] A neutral solution at exactly pH 7.0 that cannot change pH under any circumstances
    [ ] A physical barrier separating acidic and basic compartments within a cell to prevent mixing
    Explanation: Buffers contain a weak acid/conjugate base pair that absorb excess H⁺ or OH⁻. The blood's bicarbonate buffer system is vital: CO₂ + H₂O ⇌ H₂CO₃ ⇌ H⁺ + HCO₃⁻. When blood becomes too acidic, HCO₃⁻ absorbs H⁺; when too basic, H₂CO₃ releases H⁺. This system is regulated by lungs (CO₂ exhalation) and kidneys (HCO₃⁻ reabsorption). Other biological buffers include the phosphate buffer system (important in intracellular fluid) and protein buffers (hemoglobin, amino acid side chains). Blood pH outside 6.8-7.8 is lethal — demonstrating buffers' life-critical importance.

  Q21. Water (H₂O) has extraordinary properties: high specific heat (4.18 J/g·°C), high heat of vaporization (2,260 J/g), surface tension, cohesion, adhesion, and the anomalous property of being less dense as a solid than a liquid. All these properties trace back to hydrogen bonding. A coastal redwood tree (Sequoia sempervirens) can transport water 115+ meters from roots to crown without any mechanical pump. Analyze how water's hydrogen bond-derived properties make this feat possible.
    [ ] Redwood trees use active transport pumps at every branch node to push water upward against gravity, unrelated to water's chemical properties following conventional understanding
    [ ] Water rises in trees purely through osmotic pressure generated by salts in the root cells, with no role for hydrogen bonding, particularly regarding analog in accordance with classical theory
    [*] Water transport in tall trees relies on the cohesion-tension theory: transpiration from leaves creates negative pressure (tension), water's cohesion (hydrogen bonds between water molecules) maintains a continuous water column despite gravity's pull, and adhesion (hydrogen bonds between water and xylem vessel walls) helps counteract gravity — all possible because hydrogen bonds give water these unique intermolecular attractions
    [ ] Capillary action alone is sufficient to explain water movement to any height in any plant, regardless of the tree's size, particularly regarding cohesion when evaluated systematically
    Explanation: The cohesion-tension theory (Dixon & Joly, 1894) explains xylem water transport: (1) Transpiration pull — evaporation from leaf stomata creates negative pressure up to -2 MPa. (2) Cohesion — hydrogen bonds between water molecules create a tensile strength of ~25 MPa in narrow xylem vessels, maintaining an unbroken water column. (3) Adhesion — hydrogen bonds between water and hydrophilic xylem cell walls (cellulose) counteract gravity. (4) Root pressure — osmotic uptake provides modest push (~0.1-0.5 MPa). The cohesion-tension mechanism can theoretically lift water ~130 meters — matching the tallest known redwoods (115.7 m). If water lacked hydrogen bonding (like H₂S, its chemical analog), it would be a gas at room temperature and life as we know it would be impossible.

  Q22. Carbon-14 (¹⁴C) is a radioactive isotope with a half-life of 5,730 years that is continuously produced in the atmosphere by cosmic ray bombardment of nitrogen-14. Living organisms maintain the same ¹⁴C/¹²C ratio as the atmosphere through constant carbon exchange, but this ratio starts declining at death as ¹⁴C decays. In 1991, hikers in the Alps discovered 'Ötzi the Iceman' — the mummified body of a Copper Age man. Radiocarbon dating determined he died ~5,300 years ago. Explain how isotopes make this dating possible.
    [*] After death, organisms stop exchanging carbon with the environment — the radioactive ¹⁴C decays to ¹⁴N at a known rate (half-life 5,730 years) while stable ¹²C remains constant, so measuring the ¹⁴C/¹²C ratio reveals elapsed time since death. Ötzi's ¹⁴C had undergone approximately one half-life of decay (~5,300 years), meaning roughly half his original ¹⁴C had converted to nitrogen-14
    [ ] Scientists compared the color of Ötzi's bones to a standard reference chart that correlates bone darkness with age, independent of atomic properties
    [ ] Radiocarbon dating works by measuring the total carbon content of a sample — older samples simply have less total carbon because carbon physically evaporates over time
    [ ] Ötzi's age was determined by counting tree rings in wood artifacts found nearby, not by radiocarbon dating of his body
    Explanation: Radiocarbon dating (developed by Willard Libby, 1949 — won Nobel Prize 1960) exploits the radioactive decay of ¹⁴C: (1) Cosmic rays create ¹⁴C in the atmosphere: ¹⁴N + neutron → ¹⁴C + proton. (2) ¹⁴C is incorporated into CO₂, then into organisms through photosynthesis and food chains. (3) At death, ¹⁴C intake stops; existing ¹⁴C decays via beta emission: ¹⁴C → ¹⁴N + β⁻. (4) Half-life = 5,730 years, so after 1 half-life, 50% remains; after 2, 25%; etc. The method is reliable to ~50,000 years (~9 half-lives). For Ötzi (died ~3300 BCE), roughly 60% of original ¹⁴C remained. Modern AMS (accelerator mass spectrometry) requires only milligram samples. Other radioisotopes date different timescales: K-40/Ar-40 for millions of years (used to date hominin fossils).

  Q23. Sickle cell disease results from a single nucleotide mutation in the hemoglobin beta-globin gene: the sixth amino acid changes from glutamic acid (hydrophilic, charged) to valine (hydrophobic, nonpolar). This seemingly minor change at the atomic level causes hemoglobin molecules to polymerize into rigid fibers, distorting red blood cells into a sickle shape. Analyze how this case illustrates the biological significance of chemical bonding and molecular interactions.
    [ ] The disease occurs because valine forms ionic bonds with oxygen, preventing hemoglobin from carrying oxygen molecules to tissues, particularly regarding into following conventional understanding
    [*] The Glu→Val substitution replaces a charged, hydrophilic amino acid with a nonpolar, hydrophobic one — creating a 'sticky' hydrophobic patch on hemoglobin's surface that enables Van der Waals interactions and hydrophobic bonding between adjacent HbS molecules, causing polymerization into rigid fibers that distort RBCs. This demonstrates how a single change in weak intermolecular forces (not covalent bonds) can cause a devastating disease
    [ ] Sickle cell disease is caused by the extra hydrogen bonds that valine forms compared to glutamic acid, since valine has more electronegative atoms following the established framework
    [ ] The sickle cell mutation only affects the covalent bonds within the hemoglobin molecule, breaking its peptide backbone and causing it to fall apart under normal circumstances
    Explanation: Sickle cell disease (HbS) perfectly illustrates how bond types determine biological function: (1) The primary structure change is a single covalent bond difference (glutamic acid → valine at position 6). (2) Glutamic acid's R-group is -CH₂-CH₂-COO⁻ (charged, hydrophilic); valine's is -CH(CH₃)₂ (nonpolar, hydrophobic). (3) The exposed hydrophobic valine creates a 'sticky patch' that interacts via Van der Waals forces and hydrophobic interactions with a complementary pocket on adjacent deoxygenated HbS molecules. (4) This triggers polymerization into rigid fibers under low-oxygen conditions. (5) Result: distorted RBCs → vaso-occlusion → pain crises, organ damage. This cascade — from one amino acid change to whole-organism disease — demonstrates that all levels of biological organization are built on fundamental atomic and molecular interactions.

  Q24. Since the Industrial Revolution, ocean pH has decreased from ~8.2 to ~8.1 due to increased atmospheric CO₂ being absorbed by seawater: CO₂ + H₂O → H₂CO₃ → H⁺ + HCO₃⁻. This 0.1 pH unit decrease represents a 26% increase in H⁺ concentration. Marine organisms like corals and shellfish that build CaCO₃ skeletons are particularly affected. Analyze how basic chemistry concepts from this lesson explain ocean acidification and its biological impacts.
    [ ] The decrease from pH 8.2 to 8.1 means the ocean has become basic rather than acidic, so the term 'acidification' is scientifically incorrect in the context of this analysis
    [ ] A 0.1 pH change is biologically insignificant because the ocean is so vast that local chemistry cannot change measurably, particularly regarding that in accordance with classical theory
    [*] Because pH is logarithmic, even the 0.1 unit decrease represents a 26% increase in H⁺ concentration — excess H⁺ ions react with carbonate ions (CO₃²⁻) to form bicarbonate (HCO₃⁻), reducing the carbonate saturation state (Ω) that shell-building organisms need to form calcium carbonate (CaCO₃) — demonstrating how the equilibrium chemistry of CO₂ dissolution, acid-base reactions, and ionic interactions directly determines the fate of marine ecosystems
    [ ] Ocean acidification only affects the ocean's surface layer and cannot penetrate below 10 meters due to water pressure preventing CO₂ absorption based on the prevailing evidence
    Explanation: Ocean acidification integrates several chemistry concepts: (1) CO₂ dissolution and acid formation: CO₂ + H₂O → H₂CO₃ (carbonic acid). (2) Dissociation: H₂CO₃ → H⁺ + HCO₃⁻ → 2H⁺ + CO₃²⁻. (3) pH logarithmic scale: 0.1 decrease = 26% more H⁺ (10^0.1 ≈ 1.26). (4) Ionic equilibrium: excess H⁺ combines with CO₃²⁻, reducing carbonate availability. (5) Impact on biomineralization: organisms like corals (aragonite), oysters, and pteropods need sufficient carbonate saturation (Ω > 1) to build CaCO₃ shells. Below Ω = 1, shells dissolve. Projections: pH may reach 7.7-7.8 by 2100 (RCP8.5), a 150% H⁺ increase from pre-industrial levels. Pteropod shell dissolution is already observed in the Southern Ocean.

  Q25. The blood bicarbonate buffer system maintains blood pH between 7.35 and 7.45 despite the body's constant production of metabolic acids (~15,000 mmol CO₂ and ~50-100 mEq non-volatile acids daily). A patient arrives at the emergency room with uncontrolled diabetes and blood pH of 7.1 (diabetic ketoacidosis — DKA). Analyze how buffer chemistry, respiratory compensation, and renal compensation work together to restore pH.
    [ ] Blood pH is corrected exclusively by the kidneys filtering out excess hydrogen atoms from the bloodstream, with no involvement from the lungs or chemical buffers
    [ ] The blood simply dilutes the acid by increasing water content through increased thirst, reducing H⁺ concentration through volume expansion alone
    [*] The bicarbonate buffer system (CO₂ + H₂O ⇌ H₂CO₃ ⇌ H⁺ + HCO₃⁻) provides immediate chemical buffering as HCO₃⁻ absorbs excess H⁺, respiratory compensation occurs within minutes (hyperventilation/Kussmaul breathing expels CO₂, shifting equilibrium left to consume H⁺), and renal compensation over hours-days (kidneys excrete H⁺ and reabsorb HCO₃⁻) — demonstrating how acid-base equilibrium chemistry directly controls whole-organism physiology
    [ ] The body has no mechanism to correct blood pH once it falls below 7.35, and DKA is always immediately fatal without artificial buffer injection
    Explanation: DKA illustrates buffer chemistry in action: (1) Chemical cause — insulin deficiency forces ketone body production (acetoacetic acid, β-hydroxybutyric acid), flooding blood with H⁺. (2) Buffer response — HCO₃⁻ + H⁺ → H₂CO₃ → CO₂ + H₂O (HCO₃⁻ consumed, may drop from normal 22-26 mEq/L to <10). (3) Respiratory compensation — chemoreceptors detect low pH → stimulate respiratory center → Kussmaul breathing (deep, rapid) → CO₂ exhaled → equilibrium shifts left → pH rises. (4) Renal compensation (slowest) — proximal tubule cells excrete H⁺ via Na⁺/H⁺ exchangers, regenerate HCO₃⁻. (5) Treatment: IV insulin (stops ketogenesis), IV fluids, and bicarbonate (if pH <6.9). This case shows how Le Chatelier's principle governs physiology.

  Q26. Water is often called the 'universal solvent' because its polar nature allows it to dissolve more substances than any other common liquid. However, lipids (fats and oils) do not dissolve in water. Cell membranes exploit this property through the phospholipid bilayer, where hydrophilic heads face water and hydrophobic tails face inward. Explain how electronegativity, polarity, and bonding types create this essential biological structure.
    [ ] Cell membranes form because all biological molecules are equally repelled by water and spontaneously arrange into layers to minimize contact, particularly regarding multiple following the established framework
    [ ] Phospholipids are held in bilayer formation exclusively by covalent bonds between adjacent molecules, not by any interaction with water, particularly regarding cellular when considering all relevant factors
    [ ] The phospholipid bilayer forms because of ionic bonds between the positively charged phosphate heads and the negatively charged fatty acid tails as typically measured in practice
    [*] Water's polarity (from O-H electronegativity difference of 1.24 and 104.5° bond angle) allows it to form hydrogen bonds with and dissolve ionic/polar substances — but repels nonpolar hydrocarbon chains (hydrophobic effect) — causing phospholipids to self-assemble into bilayers where polar phosphate heads hydrogen-bond with water while nonpolar fatty acid tails cluster together to minimize water contact, creating the selectively permeable barrier essential for cellular life
    Explanation: The phospholipid bilayer demonstrates multiple bonding concepts: (1) Electronegativity → water's polarity: O (3.44) vs H (2.20) creates polar O-H bonds; bent geometry (104.5°) prevents dipole cancellation. (2) 'Like dissolves like': polar water dissolves polar/ionic substances but not nonpolar hydrocarbons. (3) Hydrophobic effect: nonpolar molecules in water force water to form ordered cages (decreased entropy → unfavorable); clustering hydrophobic molecules together releases these ordered water molecules (entropy increase drives self-assembly). (4) The phospholipid bilayer — hydrophilic heads (polar phosphate, ionic charges) face water; hydrophobic tails (nonpolar C-H chains) face each other — forms spontaneously, requiring no cellular energy. This thermodynamically favorable self-assembly may have been crucial for the origin of life.

  Q27. Carbon's ability to form 4 covalent bonds leads to incredible molecular diversity. Glucose (C₆H₁₂O₆) and fructose (C₆H₁₂O₆) have the identical molecular formula but completely different structures and biological properties — glucose is a six-membered ring (pyranose) while fructose is a five-membered ring (furanose). This phenomenon of structural isomerism extends to much larger molecules like the 20 amino acids that build all proteins. Analyze carbon's unique bonding properties and their biological implications.
    [ ] Carbon can only form four single bonds in a straight line, limiting molecular diversity to simple chain structures, particularly regarding versatility as typically measured in practice
    [*] Carbon's tetravalence (4 valence electrons) allows single, double, and triple bonds with multiple elements, creating chains, branches, and rings — combined with the ability to form diverse functional groups (-OH, -NH₂, -COOH, -SH, -PO₄), this generates virtually unlimited structural isomers from the same molecular formula, and the specific 3D arrangement of atoms determines biological function (e.g., glucose vs fructose recognized by different enzymes despite identical formulae)
    [ ] All molecules with the same molecular formula have identical biological properties because their atoms are the same regardless of arrangement, particularly regarding ability when applying fundamental principles
    [ ] Silicon could equally replace carbon as life's backbone element since it also has 4 valence electrons and forms comparable bonds, particularly regarding disulfide as observed in standard experiments
    Explanation: Carbon's versatility underpins molecular diversity: (1) Bond types — C-C (346 kJ/mol), C=C (614 kJ/mol), C≡C (839 kJ/mol) allow varied chain flexibility. (2) Structural isomers — same formula, different connectivity (glucose vs fructose). (3) Geometric isomers — different arrangement around double bonds (cis vs trans fatty acids: cis-oleic acid is heart-healthy, trans fats associated with cardiovascular disease). (4) Enantiomers — mirror-image isomers (L-amino acids vs D-amino acids: life uses exclusively L-amino acids and D-sugars). (5) Functional groups determine chemical behavior: -OH (alcohols, sugars), -NH₂ (amino acids), -COOH (fatty acids), -SH (disulfide bonds in proteins), -PO₄ (ATP, DNA). Silicon, though tetravalent, forms weaker Si-Si bonds (226 kJ/mol) and its oxides are solid (SiO₂ = sand) unlike gaseous CO₂, making it unsuitable for life chemistry.

  Q28. Cystic fibrosis (CF), the most common lethal genetic disease in Caucasians (~1/2,500 births), is caused by mutations in the CFTR protein — a chloride ion (Cl⁻) channel in epithelial cell membranes. The most common mutation (ΔF508) deletes a single phenylalanine amino acid, causing the protein to misfold and fail to reach the cell surface. Without functional Cl⁻ channels, thick mucus accumulates in lungs, pancreas, and intestines. Analyze how ionic interactions at the atomic level connect to this whole-organism disease.
    [ ] CF is caused by excess chloride in the body that crystallizes in tissues, completely unrelated to ion channels or membrane transport, particularly regarding also under normal circumstances
    [*] CFTR normally allows selective Cl⁻ transport across membranes, which drives osmotic water movement and maintains hydrated mucus — the ΔF508 mutation causes misfolding (disrupting hydrogen bonds and Van der Waals forces in protein tertiary structure), preventing membrane insertion, blocking Cl⁻ transport, reducing osmotic water secretion, and producing dehydrated, thick mucus that traps bacteria and obstructs airways — connecting atomic-level ion flow to organ-level pathology
    [ ] CF only affects chloride movement and has no connection to water transport, sodium ions, or the physical properties of mucus, particularly regarding hydrophobic in the context of this analysis
    [ ] The CFTR protein is an enzyme that breaks down mucus, and mutations simply reduce mucus digestion without involving ion transport, particularly regarding from under normal circumstances
    Explanation: CF demonstrates atomic-to-organism connections: (1) Molecular level — CFTR is an ATP-gated Cl⁻ channel; the ΔF508 mutation disrupts protein folding (hydrogen bonds, hydrophobic interactions, Van der Waals forces maintain 3D structure). (2) Cellular level — misfolded CFTR is tagged by ubiquitin for proteasomal degradation before reaching the cell surface. (3) Tissue level — without Cl⁻ secretion, the electrochemical gradient also reduces Na⁺ and water movement (via ENaC and osmosis), dehydrating the airway surface liquid from normal ~7μm depth to <3μm. (4) Organ level — thick mucus impairs mucociliary clearance, creating a bacterial breeding ground (Pseudomonas aeruginosa). (5) Treatment advances: ivacaftor (potentiator for G551D mutation) and elexacaftor/tezacaftor/ivacaftor (Trikafta, for ΔF508) — designed based on atomic-level understanding of CFTR protein structure.

  Q29. Geckos can climb vertical glass surfaces and even hang upside down from ceilings, supporting their entire body weight using Van der Waals forces alone. Each gecko foot has ~500,000 setae (hair-like structures), each splitting into 100-1,000 spatulae (~200 nm tips) that maximize molecular contact with surfaces. This has inspired biomimetic adhesive materials in engineering. Evaluate how this example connects fundamental intermolecular forces to macroscopic biological function and technology.
    [ ] Gecko adhesion only works on rough surfaces where mechanical interlocking occurs, and fails completely on smooth glass, particularly regarding total in accordance with classical theory
    [*] Gecko adhesion is a remarkable demonstration of how individually negligible Van der Waals forces (~0.4-4 kJ/mol) become powerful when multiplied across billions of nanoscale contact points — each spatula generates ~10 nN of adhesion, but the ~2 billion spatulae on all four feet can collectively support ~40× the gecko's weight, proving that the cumulative effect of weak intermolecular forces drives both biology and bioinspired engineering (synthetic gecko tape, surgical adhesives, space robot grippers)
    [ ] Gecko adhesion relies on a biological glue secreted from their foot pads, not on any physical or chemical intermolecular forces, particularly regarding adhesive when considering all relevant factors
    [ ] Geckos use electrostatic charges on their feet to attract surfaces, similar to how a balloon sticks to a wall after rubbing, particularly regarding applications when considering all relevant factors
    Explanation: Gecko adhesion (Autumn et al., 2000, Nature) demonstrates Van der Waals forces at the biological scale: (1) Physical mechanism — spatulae tips (<200 nm) conform to surface irregularities at the nanometer scale, maximizing intermolecular contact area. (2) Force generation — Van der Waals attraction (London dispersion forces from temporary dipoles) at each contact point is tiny (~10 nN per spatula), but ~2 billion spatulae generate ~20-40N total force. (3) Easy detachment — geckos peel toes at a 30° angle, reducing contact area and adhesion instantly. (4) Self-cleaning — the sticky system doesn't accumulate dirt because Van der Waals forces between dirt and the surface exceed those between dirt and spatulae. (5) Biomimicry applications: Geckskin (holds 700 lbs on glass), surgical adhesive (wet tissue bonding), and robotic grippers (NASA's LEMUR robot for spacecraft inspection).

  Q30. Heavy metals like lead (Pb) and mercury (Hg) are toxic even in trace amounts because they disrupt protein structure and enzyme function. Lead exposure (from old paint, contaminated water — as in the Flint, Michigan water crisis of 2014) causes neurological damage especially in children. Mercury (from contaminated fish — as in Minamata disease, Japan, 1956) causes severe nervous system damage. Analyze how these metals interfere with chemical bonding in biological systems.
    [ ] Heavy metal toxicity only affects adults because children's faster metabolism rapidly detoxifies lead and mercury before they cause damage following conventional understanding
    [*] Heavy metals disrupt biology by binding to sulfhydryl groups (-SH) of cysteine residues (forming strong Pb-S or Hg-S bonds that replace normal disulfide bridges S-S), displacing essential metal cofactors (Pb²⁺ replaces Ca²⁺ and Zn²⁺; Hg²⁺ replaces Se in selenoenzymes), and denaturing protein tertiary/quaternary structure — demonstrating how incorrect ionic and covalent interactions at the atomic level can collapse entire biological systems
    [ ] These metals are only toxic when ingested in their pure metallic form and have no effect as dissolved ions in water or food, particularly regarding especially within the typical range of values
    [ ] Heavy metals are toxic simply because they are physically large atoms that block blood vessels and nerves through their sheer size, particularly regarding coordination based on established principles
    Explanation: Heavy metal toxicity illustrates chemical bonding principles: (1) Sulfhydryl targeting — Pb²⁺ and Hg²⁺ have very high affinity for -SH groups (cysteine), forming stable metal-sulfur bonds that are stronger than normal S-S disulfide bridges, permanently disrupting protein folding. (2) Molecular mimicry — Pb²⁺ mimics Ca²⁺ (similar ionic radius), entering neurons through calcium channels, disrupting neurotransmitter release and synaptic function (explaining children's developmental neurotoxicity). (3) Enzyme inhibition — lead inhibits δ-aminolevulinic acid dehydratase (ALAD) in heme synthesis, causing anemia. (4) Bioaccumulation — methylmercury (CH₃Hg⁺) biomagnifies through food chains (Minamata: factory discharged mercury → methylated by bacteria → fish → humans). (5) Chelation therapy (EDTA, DMSA) works by providing multiple electron-pair donor groups that form stronger coordination bonds with the metal than the biological targets, literally pulling metals away from proteins.
