Carbon's ability to form 4 covalent bonds enables the structural diversity needed for life. These four classes make up ~90% of a cell's dry mass. The term 'macromolecule' was coined by Hermann Staudinger (Nobel Prize 1953), who proved that polymers are long-chain molecules rather than aggregates of small molecules.

  Q2. What is the difference between dehydration synthesis (condensation) and hydrolysis, and why are these reactions fundamental to all living organisms?
    [ ] Dehydration synthesis only occurs in plants during photosynthesis, while hydrolysis only occurs in animals during digestion
    [ ] Both reactions add water to molecules — dehydration synthesis adds water slowly while hydrolysis adds it quickly
    [ ] These reactions only apply to proteins and have no role in carbohydrate or nucleic acid metabolism
    [*] Dehydration synthesis joins monomers into polymers by removing a water molecule (–H from one + –OH from another → H₂O), while hydrolysis breaks polymers into monomers by adding water across the bond — these are the universal building and breaking reactions for all biological macromolecules
    Explanation: These paired reactions are the universal mechanism for polymer assembly and disassembly: (1) Dehydration synthesis (condensation): monomer-OH + H-monomer → monomer-monomer + H₂O. Requires energy input (endergonic). Examples: glucose + glucose → maltose + H₂O (glycosidic bond); amino acid + amino acid → dipeptide + H₂O (peptide bond); nucleotide + nucleotide → dinucleotide + H₂O (phosphodiester bond). (2) Hydrolysis (Greek: hydro = water, lysis = break): polymer + H₂O → monomers. Releases energy (exergonic). Examples: digestion of starch by amylase, protein breakdown by proteases, DNA degradation by nucleases. (3) Enzymes catalyze both directions — specific enzymes for synthesis (synthases, polymerases) and breakdown (hydrolases, lipases, proteases). Every cell uses these reactions continuously.

  Q3. What are carbohydrates, and how do monosaccharides, disaccharides, and polysaccharides differ in structure and function?
    [ ] Carbohydrates are inorganic minerals — monosaccharides are metals, disaccharides are alloys, and polysaccharides are rocks, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [*] Carbohydrates are organic molecules with C, H, and O — monosaccharides are single sugars for quick energy; disaccharides are two linked sugars; polysaccharides are long chains of many sugars serving as energy storage (starch, glycogen) or structural support (cellulose, chitin)
    [ ] Carbohydrates are made of amino acids and only function as enzymes in cells, as governed by the molecular machinery of gene expression, protein synthesis, and intracellular transport within living cells overall
    [ ] All carbohydrates are identical in structure — the terms monosaccharide, disaccharide, and polysaccharide refer to different colors of the same molecule, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Carbohydrate hierarchy: (1) Monosaccharides — single sugar units (3-7 carbons). Glucose (C₆H₁₂O₆) is the primary cellular fuel; fructose is the sweetest natural sugar; galactose is in dairy products. Can exist as ring or chain forms. (2) Disaccharides — two monosaccharides joined by glycosidic bonds via dehydration synthesis. Sucrose (glucose + fructose) = table sugar; lactose (glucose + galactose) = milk sugar; maltose (glucose + glucose) = malt sugar. (3) Polysaccharides — hundreds to thousands of monosaccharides. Storage: starch (plants, α-1,4 bonds, helical), glycogen (animals, highly branched, stored in liver and muscle). Structural: cellulose (plants, β-1,4 bonds, unbranched, most abundant organic molecule on Earth ~1.5 trillion tons), chitin (arthropod exoskeletons, fungal cell walls). Humans cannot digest cellulose (lack cellulase enzyme) — it passes as dietary fiber.

  Q4. What are lipids, and why are they classified differently from other macromolecules?
    [ ] Lipids are a type of carbohydrate that contains extra nitrogen atoms and forms true polymers from identical monomers, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] Lipids are identical to proteins except they contain more nitrogen and sulfur atoms, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] Lipids are only found in animal cells and have no role in plant biology, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] Lipids are hydrophobic organic molecules grouped by their physical property rather than chemical structure — they are NOT true polymers of repeating monomers — and include fats/oils, phospholipids, steroids, and waxes
    Explanation: Lipids are unique: (1) NOT true polymers — unlike carbohydrates (polymer of monosaccharides), proteins (polymer of amino acids), and nucleic acids (polymer of nucleotides), lipids are a diverse group defined by hydrophobicity. (2) Types and functions: Triglycerides (fats/oils) — glycerol + 3 fatty acids; store 9 kcal/g (vs 4 kcal/g for carbs/proteins); insulate organs; cushion impacts. Phospholipids — glycerol + 2 fatty acids + phosphate group; amphipathic (hydrophilic head, hydrophobic tails); spontaneously form bilayer membranes. Steroids — 4 fused carbon rings (cholesterol backbone); cholesterol stabilizes membranes; hormones (testosterone, estrogen, cortisol). Waxes — long-chain fatty acid + long-chain alcohol; waterproof cuticles on leaves, earwax, beeswax. (3) Saturated vs unsaturated — saturated: no C=C double bonds (solid at room temp: butter); unsaturated: ≥1 C=C double bond creates kinks (liquid at room temp: olive oil). Trans fats: artificially hydrogenated unsaturated fats with trans-configuration double bonds (linked to heart disease, banned in many countries).

  Q5. What are proteins, and how does amino acid sequence determine protein structure and function?
    [ ] All proteins have the same shape regardless of their amino acid sequence, and they only function as hormones, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] Proteins are made of nucleotides and function exclusively as genetic material for inheritance, measured by comparing heritable allelic variation among individuals within geographically defined breeding populations over generations
    [ ] Proteins are simple sugars that provide quick energy, and their function is independent of their shape, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] Proteins are polymers of amino acids linked by peptide bonds — the specific sequence of amino acids dictates folding into secondary, tertiary, and quaternary structures, which determine the protein's specific function
    Explanation: Protein structure hierarchy: (1) Primary — linear amino acid sequence (determined by DNA → mRNA → ribosome). 20 amino acids with varying R-groups (nonpolar, polar, charged, aromatic). (2) Secondary — local folding into α-helices (hydrogen bonds between every 4th amino acid along backbone) and β-sheets (hydrogen bonds between parallel/antiparallel strands). (3) Tertiary — overall 3D shape from R-group interactions: hydrophobic packing, hydrogen bonds, ionic bonds, disulfide bridges (between cysteine residues), van der Waals forces. (4) Quaternary — multiple polypeptide subunits assembling (e.g., hemoglobin = 4 subunits, 2α + 2β). Functions: enzymes (catalyze reactions), structural (collagen, keratin), transport (hemoglobin, channel proteins), immune (antibodies), hormones (insulin), motor (myosin, actin). A single amino acid change can be devastating — sickle cell: Glu→Val at position 6 of β-globin. The human proteome contains ~20,000+ proteins with vastly different functions.

  Q6. What are nucleic acids, and how do DNA and RNA differ in structure and function?
    [*] Nucleic acids are polymers of nucleotides (sugar + phosphate + nitrogenous base) that store and transmit genetic information
    [ ] Nucleic acids only exist in prokaryotes and have no role in human biology across diverse biological systems and environmental contexts
    [ ] Nucleic acids are carbohydrates that store energy — DNA and RNA are identical molecules with the same sugar and bases
    [ ] Nucleic acids are lipid molecules — DNA stores fat and RNA stores cholesterol as characterized by current ecological and evolutionary research
    Explanation: Nucleic acid comparison: (1) DNA — deoxyribose sugar (missing –OH at 2′ carbon), double-stranded helix (Watson & Crick 1953, using Franklin's X-ray data), bases: adenine (A), thymine (T), guanine (G), cytosine (C); A pairs with T (2 H-bonds), G pairs with C (3 H-bonds); antiparallel strands (5′→3′ and 3′→5′); stores genetic information; human genome: ~3.2 billion base pairs. (2) RNA — ribose sugar (has –OH at 2′), typically single-stranded; bases: A, U (uracil replaces thymine), G, C. Types: mRNA (carries gene code to ribosome), tRNA (carries amino acids, anticodon), rRNA (structural/catalytic component of ribosomes), plus regulatory RNAs (miRNA, siRNA). (3) Both are polymers of nucleotides linked by phosphodiester bonds (3′→5′ direction). ATP (adenosine triphosphate) is a nucleotide that serves as the universal energy currency.

  Q7. What is the relationship between monomers and polymers, and what role does functional group chemistry play in macromolecule diversity?
    [ ] Functional groups have no effect on macromolecule properties — all polymers are chemically identical regardless of their monomer composition
    [*] Monomers are small repeating subunits that link together through covalent bonds (via dehydration synthesis) to form polymers — the chemical diversity of functional groups (hydroxyl –OH, amino –NH₂, carboxyl –COOH, phosphate –PO₄, sulfhydryl –SH) on monomers determines the properties and functions of the resulting macromolecules
    [ ] Polymers are always smaller than monomers because linking units together compresses them into smaller structures
    [ ] Monomers and polymers are unrelated — polymers form spontaneously without any connection to individual monomer units
    Explanation: Monomer-polymer relationships and functional groups: (1) Each macromolecule class has characteristic monomers: carbohydrates → monosaccharides; proteins → amino acids (20 types); nucleic acids → nucleotides. Lipids are NOT true polymers. (2) Functional groups determine chemical behavior: hydroxyl (–OH) makes molecules polar/hydrophilic (sugars, alcohols); amino (–NH₂) acts as a base, accepts H⁺; carboxyl (–COOH) acts as an acid, donates H⁺; phosphate (–PO₄³⁻) carries negative charge, links nucleotides and transfers energy (ATP); sulfhydryl (–SH) forms disulfide bridges in proteins (cysteine-cysteine); carbonyl (C=O) found in aldehydes and ketones. (3) Combinatorial diversity — 20 amino acids can form 20¹⁰⁰ ≈ 10¹³⁰ possible 100-residue proteins (more than atoms in the observable universe). (4) Isomers — same molecular formula, different structures: structural isomers (glucose vs fructose, C₆H₁₂O₆) and stereoisomers (L- vs D-amino acids).

  Q8. What are the four levels of protein structure, and why is protein folding so critical for function?
    [ ] All proteins exist as flat, linear chains and do not fold — there is only one level of protein structure, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] The four levels refer to the four types of amino acids, not to structural organization, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [*] Four levels: primary (amino acid sequence), secondary, tertiary, and quaternary — correct folding is critical because a protein's specific 3D shape determines its function, and misfolding causes diseases like Alzheimer's, Parkinson's, and prion diseases
    [ ] Protein folding is random and has no relationship to function — any shape works equally well for any task, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Protein structure levels: (1) Primary — linear amino acid sequence encoded by DNA. Even one change matters: sickle cell disease = Glu→Val at position 6 of β-globin. (2) Secondary — regular local structures: α-helix (coiled, stabilized by H-bonds every 3.6 residues); β-pleated sheet (zigzag strands, parallel or antiparallel H-bonds). (3) Tertiary — complete 3D shape from multiple forces: hydrophobic interactions (R-groups cluster inside), ionic bonds (charged R-groups), hydrogen bonds, disulfide bridges (–S–S–), van der Waals forces. (4) Quaternary — only in multi-subunit proteins: hemoglobin (α₂β₂), collagen (triple helix of 3 polypeptides). (5) Folding diseases: Alzheimer's — amyloid-β misfolding → plaques; Parkinson's — α-synuclein aggregation; prion diseases (mad cow, CJD) — PrPᶜ → PrPˢᶜ misfolding is infectious (Nobel Prize: Stanley Prusiner 1997). Chaperone proteins (Hsp70, Hsp90, GroEL/GroES) assist correct folding.

  Q9. What distinguishes saturated fats from unsaturated fats, and why does this matter for human health?
    [ ] There is no chemical difference between saturated and unsaturated fats — they are named based on their color, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] Unsaturated fats contain no carbon atoms and are therefore not organic molecules, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [*] Saturated fats have no carbon-carbon double bonds (straight chains, pack tightly, solid at room temperature — butter, lard, coconut oil), while unsaturated fats have one or more C=C double bonds (kinked chains, cannot pack tightly, liquid at room temperature — olive oil, fish oil)
    [ ] Saturated fats are liquid at room temperature while unsaturated fats are solid — the opposite of what most people believe, influenced by geographic position, prevailing wind and ocean current patterns, topographic effects, and seasonal climatic shifts
    Explanation: Fat chemistry and health: (1) Saturated — all C–C single bonds; straight chains pack tightly via van der Waals forces → solid at room temp. Sources: animal fats, dairy, coconut oil, palm oil. (2) Unsaturated — one (monounsaturated, MUFA: olive oil) or more (polyunsaturated, PUFA: fish oil, flaxseed) C=C double bonds create kinks → cannot pack tightly → liquid. Cis (natural) vs trans (artificial hydrogenation) configuration. (3) Trans fats — partial hydrogenation creates trans double bonds (straight shape like saturated fat). Health effects: raise LDL ('bad' cholesterol) + lower HDL ('good' cholesterol) → strongest dietary risk for cardiovascular disease. Banned by FDA in US (2018). (4) Essential fatty acids — omega-3 (α-linolenic acid) and omega-6 (linoleic acid) — cannot be synthesized by humans, must be obtained from diet. Critical for brain function, inflammation regulation, and cell membrane fluidity.

  Q10. What are the components of a nucleotide, and how do nucleotides link together to form nucleic acids?
    [ ] Nucleotides are made of amino acids and fatty acids linked by peptide bonds, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] Nucleotide components are unknown because DNA structure has never been determined, measured by comparing heritable allelic variation among individuals within geographically defined breeding populations over generations
    [*] Each nucleotide consists of three components: (1) a 5-carbon sugar (deoxyribose in DNA, ribose in RNA), (2) a phosphate group, and (3) a nitrogenous base (purines: adenine, guanine; pyrimidines: cytosine, thymine/uracil)
    [ ] Nucleotides are single atoms that bond through metallic bonds to form DNA and RNA crystals, involving comprehensive analysis of genomic markers and heritable phenotypic variation within and between natural populations
    Explanation: Nucleotide structure and linkage: (1) Three components — a pentose sugar (5-carbon: ribose in RNA has –OH at 2′ position; deoxyribose in DNA lacks this –OH → more chemically stable, better for long-term information storage). A phosphate group (–PO₄) — gives nucleic acids their acidic nature and negative charge. A nitrogenous base — purines (double ring: adenine A, guanine G) or pyrimidines (single ring: cytosine C, thymine T in DNA, uracil U in RNA). (2) Phosphodiester bonds — form between 3′-OH of one nucleotide's sugar and 5′-phosphate of the next, catalyzed by DNA/RNA polymerase. This creates directionality: 5′→3′ synthesis. (3) Base pairing (Chargaff's rules, 1950): A pairs with T/U (2 H-bonds), G pairs with C (3 H-bonds). This complementarity enables: DNA replication (semiconservative), transcription (DNA→RNA), and the double helix structure (antiparallel strands).

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes a carbohydrate?
    [ ] A hydrophobic molecule composed of glycerol and fatty acids that stores energy in adipose tissue, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research
    [*] An organic molecule composed of carbon, hydrogen, and oxygen — with monosaccharides as monomers — that serves as a primary energy source and structural material
    [ ] A polymer of amino acids linked by peptide bonds that catalyzes biochemical reactions, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] A double-stranded helical molecule that stores genetic information in the nucleus, involving comprehensive analysis of genomic markers and heritable phenotypic variation within and between natural populations
    Explanation: Carbohydrates (literally 'hydrated carbon': Cₙ(H₂O)ₙ) include: (1) Monosaccharides — glucose (blood sugar, primary cellular fuel), fructose (fruit sugar, sweetest natural sugar), galactose (dairy). All are C₆H₁₂O₆ isomers. (2) Disaccharides — sucrose (table sugar: glucose + fructose), lactose (milk sugar: glucose + galactose), maltose (malt sugar: glucose + glucose). (3) Polysaccharides — starch (plant storage, α-1,4 glycosidic bonds, amylose + amylopectin), glycogen (animal storage, highly branched, in liver ~100g and muscle ~400g), cellulose (structural, β-1,4 bonds, most abundant organic molecule ~1.5 × 10¹² tons on Earth — humans cannot digest it because we lack cellulase; ruminants use symbiotic bacteria), chitin (second most abundant polysaccharide). Energy: glucose → glycolysis → citric acid cycle → oxidative phosphorylation → 30-32 ATP per glucose.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes a lipid?
    [*] A diverse group of hydrophobic (water-insoluble) organic molecules, NOT true polymers — that includes fats and oils (energy storage at 9 kcal/g), phospholipids (cell membrane bilayers), steroids (cholesterol, hormones)
    [ ] A polymer of nucleotides that carries genetic instructions in a double-helix structure, as determined through molecular analysis of DNA sequence variation and allele frequency distributions across sampled populations
    [ ] A chain of amino acids folded into a specific 3D shape that functions as enzymes and structural proteins, as governed by the molecular machinery of gene expression, protein synthesis, and intracellular transport within living cells overall
    [ ] A simple sugar molecule with the formula C₆H₁₂O₆ that provides quick energy for cellular activities, derived from information theory to quantify the uncertainty in predicting the species identity of a randomly selected individual
    Explanation: Lipids are defined by solubility (hydrophobic) rather than chemical structure — making them unique among macromolecule classes. (1) Triglycerides — glycerol + 3 fatty acid chains; store 9 kcal/g (>2× carbs/proteins); form adipose tissue for insulation and energy reserves. (2) Phospholipids — glycerol + 2 fatty acids + phosphate head; amphipathic → spontaneously form bilayer membranes (the fundamental architecture of all cell membranes). (3) Steroids — 4 fused carbon rings; cholesterol (membrane fluidity regulator, precursor to all steroid hormones); testosterone, estrogen, cortisol, aldosterone, vitamin D. (4) Waxes — long-chain fatty acid + long-chain alcohol; waterproof plant cuticles, ear canal protection, beeswax. (5) Fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E, K) are lipid-associated. Lipids cannot dissolve in blood plasma — they are transported by lipoproteins (LDL, HDL, chylomicrons).

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes a protein?
    [ ] A simple carbohydrate molecule that stores energy in the form of starch and glycogen, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] A nonpolar molecule that forms the lipid bilayer of cell membranes through hydrophobic interactions, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [ ] A double-stranded molecule composed of nucleotides that encodes the genetic blueprint of organisms, involving comprehensive analysis of genomic markers and heritable phenotypic variation within and between natural populations
    [*] A polymer of amino acids linked by peptide bonds that folds into a specific three-dimensional shape — with functions including enzyme catalysis, structural support, transport, immune defense, signaling, and movement
    Explanation: Proteins (Greek: proteios = 'of primary importance') are the most functionally diverse macromolecules: (1) Structure — 20 amino acids; each has an amino group (–NH₂), carboxyl group (–COOH), H, and a unique R-group (side chain) bonded to a central carbon. Peptide bonds (C–N) link amino acids via dehydration synthesis. (2) Four structural levels: primary (sequence, ~50 to >30,000 residues), secondary (α-helix, β-sheet), tertiary (3D fold), quaternary (multi-subunit). (3) Functions: enzymes (>5,000 types — amylase, DNA polymerase, ATP synthase), structural (collagen: 25-35% of total body protein; keratin: hair, nails), transport (hemoglobin: O₂ in blood; aquaporin: water channels), immune (antibodies/immunoglobulins: IgG, IgM, IgA), hormones (insulin, growth hormone), motor (myosin + actin → muscle contraction), storage (ferritin: iron storage). The human body contains ~20,000+ distinct proteins encoded by the genome.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes a nucleic acid?
    [*] A polymer of nucleotides that stores and transmits genetic information — DNA carries the hereditary blueprint, while RNA functions in protein synthesis as mRNA, tRNA, and rRNA
    [ ] A polysaccharide composed of glucose monomers that provides structural support in plant cell walls, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] A chain of fatty acids and glycerol that insulates organisms against heat loss, based on comprehensive taxonomic inventories and ecological census data collected across geographically distinct survey regions
    [ ] A hydrophobic lipid molecule that stores energy and forms waterproof barriers on cell surfaces, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Nucleic acids (discovered by Friedrich Miescher 1869; structure by Watson & Crick 1953): (1) DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid) — double helix; bases A, T, G, C; deoxyribose sugar; stores genetic code; complementary base pairing (A=T, G≡C) enables accurate replication; human genome: ~3.2 billion bp, ~20,000 genes. (2) RNA (ribonucleic acid) — single-stranded; bases A, U, G, C; ribose sugar; types: mRNA (messenger — carries gene code to ribosome), tRNA (transfer — carries amino acids, has anticodon), rRNA (ribosomal — structural/catalytic in ribosomes), plus regulatory RNAs (miRNA, siRNA, lncRNA, CRISPR guide RNA). (3) Central dogma: DNA → (transcription) → mRNA → (translation) → protein. (4) ATP (adenosine triphosphate) is a modified nucleotide serving as universal energy currency. (5) Nucleic acids are unique: they are the only macromolecules that can self-replicate (with enzyme assistance). The 'RNA world' hypothesis proposes RNA preceded both DNA and proteins in early life.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes the monomer-polymer relationship?
    [ ] Monomers are always larger than polymers because individual units have more mass than assembled chains
    [*] A monomer is a small molecular subunit that links with other monomers through covalent bonds (via dehydration synthesis) to form a polymer — a large molecule composed of repeating monomer units — and polymers are broken back into monomers by hydrolysis (adding water to break bonds)
    [ ] Monomers and polymers are unrelated molecules that exist independently with no chemical connection to each other
    [ ] A polymer is a single atom, and a monomer is a collection of polymers grouped by ionic bonds
    Explanation: The monomer-polymer concept is fundamental to all biological macromolecules: (1) Carbohydrates: monomer = monosaccharide (glucose, fructose, galactose) → polymer = polysaccharide (starch, glycogen, cellulose). Linked by glycosidic bonds. (2) Proteins: monomer = amino acid (20 types) → polymer = polypeptide/protein. Linked by peptide bonds. (3) Nucleic acids: monomer = nucleotide → polymer = DNA/RNA strand. Linked by phosphodiester bonds. (4) Lipids: NOT true polymers (no repeating monomer units — triglycerides are 3 fatty acids on 1 glycerol; each fatty acid can be different). (5) Assembly: dehydration synthesis removes H₂O → forms bond (requires energy, catalyzed by specific enzymes). (6) Disassembly: hydrolysis adds H₂O → breaks bond (releases energy, used in digestion). (7) Term coined by Jöns Jacob Berzelius (1833); polymer science pioneered by Hermann Staudinger (Nobel 1953). (8) Biological polymer sizes range from ~50 monomers (insulin: 51 amino acids) to billions (human chromosome 1 DNA: 249 million bp).

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes dehydration synthesis (condensation reaction)?
    [*] A chemical reaction in which two monomers are covalently bonded together by removing a water molecule (–H from one monomer + –OH from the other → H₂O) — this is the universal mechanism for building biological polymers including polysaccharides, polypeptides, and polynucleotides
    [ ] The evaporation of water from a cell that causes molecules to spontaneously stick together without forming chemical bonds
    [ ] A reaction that only occurs in industrial chemistry and has no biological function
    [ ] A reaction that adds water molecules to join monomers together, creating larger polymers with water embedded inside
    Explanation: Dehydration synthesis (also called condensation) is the universal building reaction: (1) Mechanism: monomer₁-OH + H-monomer₂ → monomer₁-monomer₂ + H₂O. One water molecule is released per bond formed. (2) Examples: glycosidic bond (carbohydrates: glucose + glucose → maltose + H₂O); peptide bond (proteins: amino acid + amino acid → dipeptide + H₂O); phosphodiester bond (nucleic acids: nucleotide + nucleotide → dinucleotide + H₂O); ester bond (triglycerides: glycerol + fatty acid → monoglyceride + H₂O). (3) Energy requirement — dehydration synthesis is endergonic (requires energy input), typically coupled to ATP hydrolysis. (4) Enzyme-catalyzed: glycogen synthase (builds glycogen), ribosome/peptidyl transferase (builds proteins), DNA/RNA polymerase (builds nucleic acid strands). (5) Reverse reaction — hydrolysis — adds water to break bonds, releasing monomers. These paired reactions are the fundamental assembly/disassembly system of life.

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes hydrolysis?
    [ ] The process by which water molecules are split into hydrogen and oxygen gas by sunlight, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] The process of dissolving a substance in water without breaking any chemical bonds, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [*] A chemical reaction that breaks covalent bonds within a polymer by adding a water molecule across the bond — splitting the polymer into smaller monomers — and is the reverse of dehydration synthesis
    [ ] A reaction that removes water from polymers to make them more compact and stable, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Hydrolysis (Greek: hydro = water, lysis = break) is the universal breakdown reaction: (1) Mechanism: polymer bond + H₂O → monomer₁-OH + H-monomer₂. Water is consumed (one H₂O per bond broken). (2) Digestive examples: amylase breaks starch → maltose → glucose (glycosidic bonds); pepsin/trypsin break proteins → peptides → amino acids (peptide bonds); lipase breaks triglycerides → glycerol + fatty acids (ester bonds); nucleases break DNA/RNA → nucleotides (phosphodiester bonds). (3) Energy release — hydrolysis of ATP (ATP + H₂O → ADP + Pᵢ) releases ~7.3 kcal/mol of free energy, powering cellular work. (4) Lysosomal hydrolysis — lysosomes contain ~60 different hydrolytic enzymes that digest worn-out organelles, engulfed pathogens, and macromolecules at pH ~4.5-5.0. (5) Clinical: lactose intolerance = insufficient lactase (the hydrolytic enzyme for lactose → glucose + galactose).

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes a peptide bond?
    [ ] A glycosidic bond connecting monosaccharide units in polysaccharides like starch and cellulose
    [ ] A hydrogen bond between water molecules that creates surface tension and cohesion
    [ ] A bond between nucleotides in DNA and RNA that forms the sugar-phosphate backbone
    [*] A covalent bond formed between the amino group (–NH₂) of one amino acid and the carboxyl group (–COOH) of another through dehydration synthesis — creating the C–N linkage that forms the backbone of all proteins and polypeptides
    Explanation: The peptide bond is the defining covalent linkage of proteins: (1) Formation: amino acid₁ (–COOH) + amino acid₂ (–NH₂) → peptide bond (–CO–NH–) + H₂O. Catalyzed by the ribosome (specifically the peptidyl transferase center of the large subunit, which is actually a ribozyme — an RNA catalyst). (2) Bond character: partial double bond character due to resonance (C=O ↔ C–N), making it rigid and planar. Rotation only around bonds to the central Cα carbon (phi φ and psi ψ angles, shown on Ramachandran plot). (3) Nomenclature: 2 amino acids = dipeptide; 3-10 = oligopeptide; 10-50 = polypeptide; >50 typically called a protein. (4) Polypeptide chains have directionality: N-terminus (free amino group) to C-terminus (free carboxyl group). Ribosomes synthesize proteins N→C. (5) Peptide bonds are broken by protease enzymes (pepsin, trypsin, chymotrypsin) and hydrolysis during digestion.

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes a glycosidic bond?
    [ ] An ionic bond between metal ions and protein molecules that activates enzyme function, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [*] A covalent bond formed between monosaccharides through dehydration synthesis, linking the hydroxyl groups of adjacent sugar molecules — and the specific type of glycosidic bond determines the polysaccharide's properties and digestibility
    [ ] A hydrogen bond between complementary bases in DNA that holds the double helix together, measured by comparing heritable allelic variation among individuals within geographically defined breeding populations over generations
    [ ] A peptide bond linking amino acids in protein chains through dehydration synthesis, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Glycosidic bonds are the signature linkage of carbohydrates: (1) Formation: monosaccharide₁-OH + HO-monosaccharide₂ → glycosidic bond + H₂O. (2) Types by anomeric carbon configuration: α-glycosidic bonds (starch, glycogen — digestible by human amylase) vs β-glycosidic bonds (cellulose, chitin — NOT digestible by humans; we lack cellulase). This single difference (α vs β) has enormous biological consequences. (3) Position naming: 1,4 bond (linear chains); 1,6 bond (branch points in glycogen and amylopectin). (4) Examples: maltose = glucose-α-1,4-glucose; sucrose = glucose-α-1,2-fructose; lactose = galactose-β-1,4-glucose; cellulose = glucose-β-1,4-glucose (thousands of units, forming rigid fibrils via inter-chain H-bonds). (5) Broken by specific hydrolases: amylase (starch), maltase (maltose), lactase (lactose), sucrase (sucrose), cellulase (cellulose — only bacteria, fungi, protists).

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes an amino acid?
    [ ] A fatty acid chain that links to glycerol to form the hydrophobic core of cell membranes, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] A monosaccharide molecule like glucose or fructose that polymerizes to form structural polysaccharides, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] A nucleotide subunit consisting of a sugar, phosphate, and nitrogenous base used to build DNA and RNA, involving comprehensive analysis of genomic markers and heritable phenotypic variation within and between natural populations
    [*] The monomer of proteins — an organic molecule containing a central carbon bonded to an amino group, a carboxyl group, a hydrogen atom, and a variable R-group that determines the amino acid's chemical properties — there are 20 standard amino acids in biology
    Explanation: Amino acids (20 standard types encoded by the genetic code): (1) General structure: central Cα bonded to –NH₂ (amino, acts as base), –COOH (carboxyl, acts as acid), –H, and –R (variable side chain). At physiological pH (7.4), amino acids exist as zwitterions (–NH₃⁺ and –COO⁻). (2) R-group categories: nonpolar/hydrophobic (Gly, Ala, Val, Leu, Ile, Pro, Phe, Trp, Met — cluster in protein interior); polar/uncharged (Ser, Thr, Asn, Gln, Tyr, Cys — form H-bonds); positively charged (Lys, Arg, His — surface residues); negatively charged (Asp, Glu — surface, active sites). (3) Special amino acids: glycine (smallest, fits tight spaces), proline (rigid ring, forces turns), cysteine (–SH can form disulfide bridges), histidine (pKa ~6.0, often in enzyme active sites). (4) Essential amino acids — 9 that humans cannot synthesize (must be obtained from diet): His, Ile, Leu, Lys, Met, Phe, Thr, Trp, Val. (5) Genetic code: 61 codons specify 20 amino acids (degenerate code), plus 3 stop codons.

  Q21. SCENARIO: A hospital patient receives an IV of pure distilled water by mistake instead of normal (0.9%) saline. Within minutes, the patient's red blood cells begin to swell and burst (hemolysis), releasing hemoglobin into the plasma and potentially causing kidney damage. Red blood cells normally exist in isotonic plasma (~0.9% NaCl, ~290 mOsm/L). The cell membrane is selectively permeable — water passes freely through aquaporin channels, but ions and large molecules cannot cross without transporters. Osmotic pressure across the membrane is governed by the van't Hoff equation: π = iMRT. What macromolecule-related knowledge explains this clinical emergency?
    [ ] The hemolysis was caused by the temperature of the IV fluid, not by any osmotic effect related to macromolecules, which weights each species contribution by its proportional abundance in the sample using logarithmic transformation of count data
    [ ] The distilled water chemically destroyed the hemoglobin proteins inside the red blood cells through direct oxidation, incorporating data on the number of distinct taxa present and the statistical distribution of individual abundances among those taxa
    [*] The cell membrane is selectively permeable — in the hypotonic distilled water, osmosis drives water into the cells, causing them to swell beyond the membrane's elastic limit and lyse, releasing their protein contents (hemoglobin) into the bloodstream
    [ ] Red blood cells burst because distilled water contains toxic chemicals that dissolve the cell wall, derived from information theory to quantify the uncertainty in predicting the species identity of a randomly selected individual
    Explanation: This scenario integrates lipid (membrane), protein (aquaporin, hemoglobin), and carbohydrate (glycocalyx) knowledge: (1) Phospholipid bilayer — the selectively permeable barrier; hydrophobic core prevents free passage of ions and polar molecules. (2) Aquaporin proteins — water channel proteins that allow rapid osmotic water flow (~3 billion H₂O molecules/sec/channel; Nobel Prize: Peter Agre 2003). (3) Hemoglobin — the major intracellular protein (280 million copies per RBC, each carrying 4 O₂); when released by lysis, free hemoglobin damages renal tubules. (4) Glycocalyx — carbohydrate chains on RBC surface determine blood type (A, B, AB, O antigens are polysaccharides). (5) Clinical prevention: IV fluids must be isotonic (0.9% NaCl or 5% dextrose). Dextrose (glucose) is metabolized, leaving free water for gradual distribution. (6) The van't Hoff equation: osmotic pressure π = iMRT, where i = van't Hoff factor (NaCl → 2 ions), M = molarity, R = gas constant, T = temperature.

  Q22. SCENARIO: A marathon runner depletes glycogen stores during a 42.2 km race. She starts the race with ~500g of total glycogen (100g in liver, 400g in muscles), providing ~2,000 kcal of energy. The marathon requires ~2,600 kcal. After 90 minutes (approximately 30 km), her accessible glycogen reserves are nearly depleted — she experiences sudden fatigue, confusion, and difficulty maintaining pace. Runners call this 'hitting the wall' or 'bonking.' Her body begins metabolizing fat (lipolysis of triglycerides) and even some muscle protein as alternative fuel sources. What does this scenario reveal about the roles of different macromolecules in energy metabolism?
    [ ] The runner hits the wall because her muscles run out of water, not because of any macromolecule depletion, with specific management protocols for maintaining viable populations of native species and the ecological processes they depend on
    [*] Carbohydrates are the body's preferred quick-energy source because glucose can be rapidly mobilized and metabolized — when glycogen is depleted, the body shifts to fat metabolism and eventually protein catabolism, demonstrating the hierarchical roles of different macromolecules in energy supply
    [ ] All macromolecules provide identical energy per gram, so depleting glycogen has no effect on performance, managed according to science-based conservation guidelines and internationally recognized biodiversity protection targets and protocols
    [ ] Only nucleic acids provide energy during exercise, and carbohydrates and lipids have no role in fueling muscle contractions, designed to maintain viable native species populations and the ecological processes that sustain natural community structure long-term
    Explanation: Macromolecule energy hierarchy: (1) Carbohydrates first — glycogen → glucose → glycolysis → pyruvate → acetyl-CoA → citric acid cycle → oxidative phosphorylation. Fastest ATP production rate; brain requires ~120g glucose/day. (2) Fats second — triglycerides → glycerol + fatty acids → β-oxidation → acetyl-CoA → citric acid cycle. More energy dense (9 vs 4 kcal/g) but slower: requires more O₂ per ATP, can't fuel anaerobic bursts. Cannot cross blood-brain barrier (brain can't directly use fatty acids). (3) Proteins last resort — amino acids → deamination → carbon skeletons enter citric acid cycle (gluconeogenesis converts some to glucose). Muscle wasting during starvation. (4) 'Hitting the wall' occurs when glycogen depletes and fat oxidation can't match the ATP demand of running pace. The brain shifts to ketone bodies (from fat) as partial glucose substitute. (5) Sports nutrition: carb-loading before races maximizes glycogen storage; mid-race glucose gels delay glycogen depletion.

  Q23. SCENARIO: In 1953, James Watson and Francis Crick published 'Molecular Structure of Nucleic Acids: A Structure for Deoxyribose Nucleic Acid' — a 1-page paper in Nature that revealed the double helix structure of DNA. They relied on crucial data: Rosalind Franklin's X-ray diffraction 'Photo 51' (showing helical structure with 3.4 nm pitch and 2 nm diameter), Erwin Chargaff's base pairing rules (A=T, G=C percentages are equal in any organism's DNA), and Linus Pauling's α-helix model of proteins. The structure immediately suggested a mechanism for DNA replication ('It has not escaped our notice...'). Watson, Crick, and Maurice Wilkins received the 1962 Nobel Prize; Franklin had died of ovarian cancer in 1958 (possibly from X-ray exposure). How does DNA's macromolecular structure enable its function as the genetic blueprint?
    [ ] The double helix was a theoretical model that has since been disproved — DNA is actually a flat, ribbon-like molecule, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [ ] DNA's structure is irrelevant to its function — any polymer could store genetic information equally well regardless of its shape, as governed by the molecular machinery of gene expression, protein synthesis, and intracellular transport within living cells overall
    [ ] DNA stores information through its phospholipid content, not through the sequence of nitrogenous bases, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [*] The double helix structure directly enables DNA's functions: (1) complementary base pairing (A-T, G-C) allows accurate replication by using each strand as a template, (2) the sugar-phosphate backbone provides structural integrity, (3) the sequence of bases encodes genetic information
    Explanation: Structure-function relationship of DNA: (1) Complementary base pairing — A=T (2 H-bonds), G≡C (3 H-bonds). This creates redundancy: each strand contains all information needed to reconstruct the other (semiconservative replication, confirmed by Meselson-Stahl 1958). (2) Antiparallel strands — 5′→3′ and 3′→5′ directions. DNA polymerase reads 3′→5′, synthesizes 5′→3′. Leading strand: continuous. Lagging strand: Okazaki fragments. (3) Information coding — the sequence of bases (4-letter alphabet: A, T, G, C) encodes protein sequences through triplet codons (4³ = 64 codons → 20 amino acids + 3 stop codons). (4) Stability — the hydrophobic base stacking interactions (van der Waals) contribute more to helix stability than H-bonds between bases. GC-rich DNA is more stable than AT-rich (3 vs 2 H-bonds). (5) Accessibility — the major groove (wider) and minor groove (narrower) allow regulatory proteins (transcription factors) to read the base sequence without unwinding the helix.

  Q24. SCENARIO: A child is diagnosed with celiac disease — an autoimmune condition triggered by gluten proteins found in wheat, barley, and rye. When the child eats bread, gliadin (a component of gluten) partially resists digestion by human proteases, producing immunogenic peptide fragments. These fragments trigger an immune response in the small intestine, causing inflammation and destruction of intestinal villi (finger-like projections that increase surface area for nutrient absorption by ~600×). With damaged villi, the child cannot properly absorb nutrients, leading to malnutrition, growth stunting, and diarrhea. The tissue transglutaminase enzyme modifies gliadin peptides, increasing their binding to HLA-DQ2/DQ8 molecules on antigen-presenting cells. Treatment is a lifelong gluten-free diet. How does this scenario illustrate the relationship between protein structure, enzyme function, and macromolecule digestion?
    [ ] The child's immune system attacks bread because carbohydrates in wheat are toxic to all humans
    [*] Gluten (a protein) resists complete hydrolysis by digestive proteases due to its proline-rich structure
    [ ] Celiac disease is caused by a carbohydrate allergy, not a protein-mediated immune response
    [ ] Celiac disease damages the liver, not the intestines, and has no connection to protein digestion
    Explanation: Celiac disease illustrates multiple macromolecule concepts: (1) Protein structure — gliadin is unusually rich in proline and glutamine residues, which make it resistant to complete proteolytic hydrolysis (proline introduces rigid kinks that human proteases, especially pepsin and trypsin, cut poorly). (2) Enzyme specificity — human digestive proteases have defined substrate specificities; they cannot fully break down all dietary proteins. (3) Protein-protein interaction — tissue transglutaminase (an enzyme) deamidates glutamine→glutamate in gliadin peptides, creating negatively charged residues with high affinity for the peptide-binding groove of HLA-DQ2/DQ8 proteins (genetically determined). (4) Immune response — T cells recognize the gliadin-HLA complex as foreign → inflammation → villous atrophy → malabsorption. (5) Macromolecule digestion failure — normally, proteins are fully hydrolyzed to free amino acids and dipeptides. ~1% of the population has celiac disease (genetic predisposition + gluten exposure), making it one of the most common autoimmune conditions.

  Q25. SCENARIO: Trans fats were created through industrial partial hydrogenation — bubbling hydrogen gas through liquid vegetable oils with a nickel catalyst under heat and pressure. This process converted some cis (natural) double bonds in unsaturated fatty acids to trans configuration, creating fats that were solid at room temperature, had longer shelf life, and were cheaper than butter. Crisco (1911) and margarine became staples. By the 1990s, studies linked trans fat consumption to dramatically increased cardiovascular disease risk — trans fats raise LDL ('bad' cholesterol) AND lower HDL ('good' cholesterol), doubling the harm of saturated fats. The FDA estimated that eliminating trans fats from the US food supply would prevent 10,000-20,000 heart attacks per year. In 2018, the FDA banned artificial trans fats. What does this scenario demonstrate about the relationship between lipid molecular structure and biological effect?
    [ ] All fats are equally harmful regardless of their molecular configuration — the distinction between saturated, cis, and trans is medically meaningless, as driven by long-term patterns in solar radiation, atmospheric circulation, precipitation, and seasonal temperature variation
    [ ] The molecular geometry of lipids has no effect on human health — trans fats and cis fats are biologically identical, shaped by regional patterns in temperature, precipitation, prevailing winds, and the interactions between ocean and atmosphere
    [*] The cis-to-trans isomer change eliminates the natural 'kink' in unsaturated fatty acid chains, making them structurally similar to saturated fats — this seemingly minor geometric change in lipid structure profoundly affects biological function: trans fats integrate into cell membranes, alter membrane fluidity, increase LDL/decrease HDL, promote inflammation
    [ ] Trans fats are dangerous only because they contain toxic metals from the nickel catalyst, not because of their molecular structure, influenced by geographic position, prevailing wind and ocean current patterns, topographic effects, and seasonal climatic shifts
    Explanation: Trans fat biology: (1) Molecular geometry — cis double bonds create a 30° kink in fatty acid chain; trans double bonds produce a nearly straight chain (like saturated fat). Same molecular formula, profoundly different 3D shape. (2) Membrane effects — trans fatty acids incorporate into phospholipid bilayers, reducing membrane fluidity (similar to cholesterol) and altering the function of membrane proteins (receptors, channels). (3) Cholesterol metabolism — trans fats increase LDL production (liver produces more VLDL), decrease LDL receptor expression (impaired clearance), AND decrease HDL (reverse cholesterol transport reduced). Saturated fat only raises LDL; trans fats do both → 2× cardiovascular risk per calorie. (4) Inflammation — trans fats increase C-reactive protein, TNF-α, and IL-6 (inflammatory markers). (5) Natural trans fats — ruminant animals produce small amounts of trans-vaccenic acid (converted to CLA) via bacterial biohydrogenation, but these appear less harmful than industrial trans fats. (6) Industrial impact: WHO estimated trans fats cause ~500,000 cardiovascular deaths/year globally.

  Q26. SCENARIO: Sickle cell disease is caused by a single nucleotide mutation in the HBB gene: adenine (A) is changed to thymine (T) at the sixth codon, changing the amino acid from glutamic acid (hydrophilic, negatively charged) to valine (hydrophobic). This one amino acid substitution (Glu6Val) in β-globin causes hemoglobin tetramers (HbS) to polymerize into rigid fibers when deoxygenated, distorting red blood cells into a sickle (crescent) shape. Sickled cells block capillaries (vaso-occlusion), causing severe pain crises, organ damage, and a shortened lifespan. Despite being devastating, the sickle cell allele persists at high frequency (~10-40%) in malaria-endemic regions because heterozygous carriers (HbAS) have significant resistance to Plasmodium falciparum malaria (heterozygote advantage). How does this illustrate the connection between nucleic acid sequence, protein structure, and biological function?
    [*] A single nucleotide change (A→T) alters one codon, changing one amino acid (Glu→Val) in the protein sequence — this switches a hydrophilic resi
    [ ] Sickle cell disease is caused by a lipid deficiency in red blood cell membranes, not by any change in DNA or protein
    [ ] One nucleotide change cannot affect protein function because genes have thousands of bases that buffer against single mutations
    [ ] The sickle shape is caused by a carbohydrate deficiency on the red blood cell surface, unrelated to hemoglobin protein
    Explanation: Sickle cell disease is the textbook example of structure-function from nucleic acid to phenotype: (1) DNA level — GAG→GTG at codon 6 of β-globin gene (chromosome 11). (2) mRNA level — codon changes from GAG (Glu) to GUG (Val). (3) Protein level — Glu (hydrophilic, charged, faces outward) replaced by Val (hydrophobic, seeks other hydrophobic surfaces). (4) Quaternary structure — the Val6 fits into a hydrophobic pocket on an adjacent HbS tetramer, initiating polymerization into long fibers (only when deoxygenated, because the hydrophobic pocket is exposed only in deoxy-HbS). (5) Cell level — rigid fibers distort the flexible biconcave disc into a rigid sickle shape. (6) Tissue level — sickled cells cannot deform to pass through narrow capillaries (5-8 μm) → vaso-occlusion → ischemia → pain crisis → organ damage. (7) Population level — heterozygote advantage in malaria-endemic regions maintains the allele at ~10-40% frequency (balanced polymorphism). (8) Treatment: hydroxyurea increases fetal hemoglobin (HbF), gene therapy with CRISPR is showing promise.

  Q27. SCENARIO: Spider silk is a protein-based material with extraordinary mechanical properties: it is 5× stronger than steel by weight, 3× tougher than Kevlar, can stretch to 140% of its length before breaking, and is produced at room temperature in water (unlike synthetic materials requiring extreme heat and toxic solvents). The major component, spidroin, contains repetitive amino acid sequences: polyalanine blocks (Ala-Ala-Ala-Ala-Ala) form β-sheet crystalline regions (providing strength), while glycine-rich sequences (Gly-Gly-X) form amorphous flexible regions (providing elasticity). A single orb weaver spider can produce 7 different types of silk, each with different protein composition optimized for different functions (dragline, capture spiral, egg sac, etc.). How does the amino acid sequence of spider silk determine its remarkable properties?
    [*] The specific amino acid sequence creates a composite structure: polyalanine blocks fold into rigid β-sheet crystals (providing tensile strength through dense hydrogen bonding networks), while glycine-rich regions form flexible amorphous chains (providing elasticity and energy absorption)
    [ ] Spider silk's properties come from inorganic mineral crystals embedded in the fiber, not from protein structure, as driven by long-term patterns in solar radiation, atmospheric circulation, precipitation, and seasonal temperature variation
    [ ] All proteins have identical mechanical properties regardless of their amino acid sequence — spider silk is just thicker than other protein fibers, shaped by regional patterns in temperature, precipitation, prevailing winds, and the interactions between ocean and atmosphere
    [ ] Spider silk is made of cellulose polysaccharides, not proteins, and its strength comes from glycosidic bonds, influenced by geographic position, prevailing wind and ocean current patterns, topographic effects, and seasonal climatic shifts
    Explanation: Spider silk demonstrates protein structure-function at its most impressive: (1) Primary structure — repetitive motifs: polyalanine (An) and glycine-rich (GGX, GPGXX) sequences. (2) Secondary structure — polyalanine forms antiparallel β-sheet crystals (hydrogen bond networks create stiff, strong domains); glycine-rich regions form 31-helices or random coils (flexible, extensible). (3) Hierarchical architecture — β-sheet nanocrystals (~2-7 nm) are embedded in an amorphous glycine-rich matrix, creating a nanocomposite. (4) Self-assembly — spidroin proteins (250-350 kDa) are stored as a concentrated liquid crystal in the silk gland (~30-50% w/v). During spinning through the spinneret, pH change (7→5), ion exchange, and shear force trigger phase transition to insoluble fiber. (5) Materials science interest — 'biomimetic' efforts to produce recombinant spider silk for sutures, bulletproof vests, artificial tendons, biodegradable materials. Companies like Bolt Threads and Spiber are commercializing recombinant silk proteins.

  Q28. SCENARIO: A biochemistry student tests four unknown biological samples using chemical indicator tests: (A) Benedict's reagent (detects reducing sugars) → color change. (B) Iodine test → dark blue-black color. (C) Biuret reagent (detects peptide bonds) → purple color. (D) Sudan IV (lipid stain) → red-stained layer. Based on these results: Sample A contains a reducing sugar (like glucose or maltose), Sample B contains starch (amylose forms a helical complex with iodine), Sample C contains protein (Cu²⁺ ions coordinate with nitrogen atoms in peptide bonds, producing violet color), and Sample D contains lipids. These are standard biochemistry lab techniques used in clinical diagnostics and food science. What does each test reveal about macromolecule structure?
    [ ] All four tests detect the same macromolecule type — the different colors are random and have no diagnostic meaning, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [*] Each test exploits a specific structural feature of a macromolecule class: Benedict's detects free aldehyde/ketone groups on reducing sugars, iodine detects the helical structure of amylose (starch) by fitting inside the coil, Biuret detects peptide bonds in proteins through copper ion coordination
    [ ] Chemical indicator tests cannot distinguish between macromolecule classes because all organic molecules react identically, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] These tests only work on purified laboratory chemicals and cannot identify macromolecules in actual biological samples, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Macromolecule identification tests and their structural basis: (1) Benedict's test — Cu²⁺ (blue) is reduced to Cu₂O (orange-red precipitate) by free aldehyde/ketone groups on open-chain sugars. Reducing sugars: glucose, fructose, maltose, lactose. Non-reducing: sucrose (no free anomeric carbon). Quantitative in clinical glucose testing. (2) Iodine-starch test — I₂/KI solution turns blue-black with amylose because iodine molecules (I₅⁻ chains) fit inside the helical coil of amylose. Amylopectin → reddish-brown (shorter helices). Glycogen → reddish-brown. Cellulose → no reaction (β-1,4 bonds don't form coils). (3) Biuret test — Cu²⁺ in alkaline solution coordinates with 4 nitrogen atoms from peptide bonds → violet/purple complex. Requires ≥2 peptide bonds (not free amino acids). More peptide bonds → deeper color. (4) Sudan IV/Sudan III — hydrophobic dye dissolves in lipid phase (like dissolves like), staining fats/oils red. Used in clinical stool fat tests (steatorrhea diagnosis).

  Q29. SCENARIO: During the Irish Potato Famine (1845-1852), the fungus-like oomycete Phytophthora infestans destroyed potato crops across Ireland, causing approximately 1 million deaths and 1 million emigrants. Potatoes were the primary food source because they are extremely calorie-dense: a single acre produces ~15 million calories of starch (vs ~6 million for wheat). Potato starch consists of amylose (~20%, linear α-1,4 glucose chains) and amylopectin (~80%, branched α-1,4 with α-1,6 branches). Human digestive enzymes (salivary and pancreatic amylase) cleave α-1,4 glycosidic bonds, eventually producing glucose for cellular respiration. Interestingly, resistant starch (in cooled cooked potatoes) becomes partially indigestible because retrograded amylose forms tightly packed crystalline structures that resist amylase. How does carbohydrate structure relate to both the famine's impact and human nutrition?
    [ ] Starch structure is irrelevant to nutrition — all carbohydrates are digested identically regardless of their molecular arrangement
    [ ] Potatoes contain no carbohydrates — their caloric value comes entirely from protein and fat content based on established principles of ecology and evolutionary biology
    [ ] The famine occurred because humans cannot digest potato starch at all — it passes through as indigestible fiber as characterized by current ecological and evolutionary research
    [*] Potato starch (α-1,4 and α-1,6 glycosidic bonds linking glucose monomers into amylose and amylopectin polymers) provided massive caloric density per acre
    Explanation: Carbohydrate structure-function in historical context: (1) Starch = plant energy storage polymer of glucose with α-glycosidic bonds: amylose (linear α-1,4, forms helices, ~20%) and amylopectin (branched α-1,4 + α-1,6, ~80%). (2) Why potatoes are calorie-dense: compact starch granules pack ~80% of dry weight as readily digestible polysaccharide. Per acre: ~15 million kcal (vs wheat ~6M, corn ~12M). (3) Human digestion: salivary α-amylase begins hydrolyzing α-1,4 bonds orally; pancreatic amylase continues in small intestine → maltose + maltotriose + α-limit dextrins → brush border enzymes (maltase, isomaltase) → glucose. (4) Resistant starch — when cooked starch cools, amylose chains realign into tight crystalline structures (retrogradation) that resist amylase. Benefits: fermented by gut microbiota → short-chain fatty acids (butyrate) → colon health. (5) α vs β bonds — humans digest α (starch, glycogen) but NOT β (cellulose, chitin) — same glucose monomer, different bond = entirely different digestibility. (6) Famine lesson: monoculture dependency on a single carbohydrate source + genetic uniformity → catastrophic food insecurity.

  Q30. SCENARIO: The origin of life required the formation of biological macromolecules from simple molecules under prebiotic conditions. The Miller-Urey experiment (1953) demonstrated that amino acids could form from simple gases (CH₄, NH₃, H₂O, H₂) when exposed to electrical sparks simulating lightning — producing glycine, alanine, aspartic acid, and other amino acids. However, forming polymers (proteins, nucleic acids) from monomers is thermodynamically unfavorable in dilute aqueous solution because dehydration synthesis releases water, which is disfavored in an environment already full of water (Le Chatelier's principle). Proposed solutions include: clay mineral surfaces catalyzing polymerization, tidal pool concentration through evaporation cycles, and deep-sea hydrothermal vents providing energy and mineral catalysts. The 'RNA world' hypothesis proposes that RNA preceded both DNA and proteins because RNA can both store information AND catalyze reactions (ribozymes — Nobel Prize: Thomas Cech & Sidney Altman, 1989). What challenges does the spontaneous formation of biological macromolecules present?
    [ ] Macromolecules cannot form without living cells — the origin of life cannot be studied scientifically because it is impossible to create organic molecules from inorganic materials, as determined through molecular analysis of DNA sequence variation and allele frequency distributions across sampled populations
    [*] The key challenge is that polymer formation via dehydration synthesis is thermodynamically unfavorable in water — so prebiotic polymer formation required special conditions: concentration of monomers, mineral surface catalysis (clay templates), energy sources, and the eventual emergence of catalytic RNA (ribozymes) that could both store information and catalyze its own replication
    [ ] All four macromolecule classes appeared simultaneously and fully formed — there was no stepwise chemical evolution, measured by comparing heritable allelic variation among individuals within geographically defined breeding populations over generations
    [ ] There are no challenges — macromolecules form spontaneously in any aqueous environment without any energy input or catalysis, involving comprehensive analysis of genomic markers and heritable phenotypic variation within and between natural populations
    Explanation: Prebiotic macromolecule formation: (1) Monomer synthesis — Miller-Urey (1953) produced amino acids, Jeffrey Bada (2008) re-analyzed sealed vials finding 20+ amino acids. Formaldehyde → simple sugars (formose reaction). HCN → adenine (Oró 1961). Nucleotides can form on mineral surfaces. (2) Polymerization challenge — in dilute aqueous solution, hydrolysis is thermodynamically favored over dehydration synthesis (ΔG > 0 for condensation in water). Solutions: wet-dry cycles (tidal pools: evaporation concentrates monomers + heat drives dehydration synthesis); clay minerals (montmorillonite catalyzes RNA polymerization up to 50+ nucleotides — Ferris 2002); deep-sea vents (FeS/NiS surfaces catalyze peptide bond formation). (3) RNA world — ribozymes discovered by Cech (self-splicing intron) and Altman (RNase P) proved RNA can be both genetic material AND catalyst. Modern ribosome is fundamentally a ribozyme (peptidyl transferase = rRNA). (4) Lipid vesicles — fatty acids spontaneously form membrane-bound compartments (protocells) in water, providing enclosed environments for chemical evolution — bridging to the first cells ~3.8 billion years ago.
