What is bioluminescence, and how do the biochemical reactions that produce biological light illustrate enzyme catalysis?
Bioluminescence biochemistry: (1) General reaction: Luciferin + O₂ → (luciferase catalyst, often ATP-dependent) → Oxyluciferin + Light. The terms luciferin/luciferase (from Latin 'lucifer' = light-bearer) are generic — different organisms evolved different luciferins independently. (2) Firefly (Photinus pyralis): D-luciferin + ATP + O₂ → (firefly luciferase) → oxyluciferin + AMP + PPi + CO₂ + light (λ 560 nm, yellow-green). Quantum yield ~0.88 (88% of reactions produce a photon — among the most efficient chemical reactions known). Used for mating signals. (3) Marine bioluminescence: coelenterazine is the most common marine luciferin (jellyfish Aequorea victoria uses it — source of GFP, Green Fluorescent Protein: Shimomura discovered aequorin, Chalfie & Tsien engineered GFP — Nobel 2008). Deep-sea anglerfish use symbiotic bioluminescent bacteria (Photobacterium). ~76% of deep-sea organisms are bioluminescent. (4) Applications: GFP revolutionized cell biology (gene expression reporter, protein localization tracking). ATP-dependent luciferase used as biosensor for contamination detection (ATP = indicator of living cells). Bioluminescence imaging in cancer research (tumor monitoring in mice). (5) Evolution: bioluminescence evolved independently at least 40-50 times across diverse taxa — bacteria, dinoflagellates, fungi, jellyfish, corals, insects, fish, squid.
Real-World Applications
Example: What are antioxidants, and how do they protect cells from damage caused by free radicals?
Antioxidants and oxidative stress: (1) Free radicals: atoms or molecules with unpaired electrons — highly reactive. Reactive oxygen species (ROS): superoxide (O₂⁻•), hydroxyl radical (•OH), hydrogen peroxide (H₂O₂), singlet oxygen. Generated by: mitochondrial electron transport chain (1-3% of O₂ becomes superoxide normally), UV radiation, ionizing radiation, cigarette smoke, pollution, some drugs. (2) Damage mechanism: lipid peroxidation (chain reaction: free radical abstracts H from unsaturated fatty acid → lipid radical → reacts with O₂ → peroxyl radical → propagates to neighboring lipids → membrane damage). Also: DNA oxidation (8-oxoguanine → G→T transversions → mutations), protein oxidation (carbonylation → loss of function, aggregation). (3) Antioxidant defense: Enzymatic — superoxide dismutase (SOD: O₂⁻• → H₂O₂), catalase (H₂O₂ → H₂O + O₂), glutathione peroxidase (reduces H₂O₂ using GSH). Non-enzymatic — vitamin C (ascorbate, water-soluble, regenerates vitamin E), vitamin E (α-tocopherol, lipid-soluble, breaks lipid peroxidation chains), glutathione (GSH, most abundant intracellular antioxidant, tripeptide), carotenoids (β-carotene, lycopene), polyphenols (from fruits, vegetables, tea, coffee). (4) Oxidative stress: imbalance between ROS production and antioxidant defense → linked to aging (free radical theory of aging — Denham Harman, 1956), cancer, cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer's, Parkinson's.
Example: A nutrition label on a packaged cookie states: 'Calories: 200, Total Fat: 10g (Saturated 3g, Trans 0g), Total Carbohydrates: 25g (Sugars 15g), Protein: 2g.' However, the ingredient list includes 'partially hydrogenated soybean oil.' A biochemistry student realizes this is misleading. Why might the label say '0g trans fat' while still containing trans fats, and how should the student evaluate this product?
Hidden trans fats in food labeling: (1) FDA labeling loophole (pre-2018 ban, still relevant for some products): manufacturers could round down to 0g if serving contained < 0.5g trans fat. Strategy: reduce serving size to get trans fat below 0.5g → label '0g trans fat.' (2) The giveaway: 'partially hydrogenated oil' in ingredients = contains trans fats. Partial hydrogenation adds some (not all) H atoms to unsaturated fatty acids, and the process inevitably isomerizes some cis double bonds to trans configuration. Fully hydrogenated oils (all double bonds eliminated → saturated) contain no trans fats. (3) Cumulative exposure: 0.49g × 4 servings = ~2g trans fat. WHO recommends < 1% of total calories from trans fat (~2.2g for 2000 kcal diet). Even small amounts increase cardiovascular risk. (4) FDA banned partially hydrogenated oils (PHOs) from US food supply (final compliance June 2018) — but some products with extended shelf lives may still contain them, and exemptions/enforcement gaps exist. (5) Global progress: Denmark first country to effectively ban (2003, ≤ 2% of total fat). WHO REPLACE initiative targets global elimination. As of 2023, ~60 countries have implemented regulations. (6) Lesson: ingredient lists > nutrition facts labels for identifying problematic substances.
Example: A marathon runner collapses at mile 20 — the infamous 'hitting the wall.' Medical staff find she is hypoglycemic (blood glucose 52 mg/dL) and barely conscious. She had eaten only a large pasta dinner the night before and skipped breakfast. Using your knowledge of biochemistry, explain what happened metabolically and how dehydration synthesis and hydrolysis relate to energy storage and release during exercise.
Marathon metabolism and the polymer-monomer connection: (1) Carb-loading: the pasta dinner provided glucose → dehydration synthesis stored it as glycogen (α-1,4 and α-1,6 glycosidic bonds). Muscle glycogen (~400g, ~1,600 kcal) for local use. Liver glycogen (~100g, ~400 kcal) for blood glucose maintenance. Total: ~2,000 kcal from glycogen. (2) Marathon energy demand: ~100 kcal/mile × 26.2 miles = ~2,620 kcal. At marathon pace, fuel is ~50-70% carbohydrate, ~30-50% fat (ratio depends on intensity and training). (3) Progressive fuel depletion: miles 1-10: glycogen + fat → plenty of ATP. Miles 10-18: glycogen declining, fat oxidation increasing. Mile ~20: glycogen essentially depleted ('the wall'). Fat oxidation cannot produce ATP fast enough to maintain pace (fat yields more total ATP per gram but at slower rate due to β-oxidation complexity). (4) Blood glucose crash: liver glycogen (maintained blood glucose) runs out → hypoglycemia → brain relies almost exclusively on glucose → confusion, dizziness, collapse. (5) Skipped breakfast: liver glycogen already depleted overnight (used to maintain blood glucose during sleep) → started race with ~50-60g liver glycogen instead of ~100g. (6) Hydrolysis is the key catabolic reaction: glycogen phosphorylase + debranching enzyme → glucose-1-phosphate → glucose-6-phosphate → glycolysis → pyruvate → mitochondria. (7) Prevention: sports nutrition recommends carb-loading 48-72 hours before, breakfast 3-4 hours before race, mid-race fueling (gels/drinks every 30-45 min to maintain blood glucose).
Example: A Type 1 diabetic teenager forgets to take his insulin before eating a large pizza. Three hours later, he is vomiting, breathing rapidly (Kussmaul respiration), and his breath smells fruity. His blood glucose reads 450 mg/dL. His mother calls 911. Explain the biochemical cascade that led to diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA), and how does this illustrate the role of insulin in everyday metabolism?
Diabetic ketoacidosis biochemistry: (1) Trigger: no insulin + carbohydrate load. Insulin normally: ↑GLUT4 translocation → ↑glucose uptake by muscle/adipose; ↑glycogenesis; ↑glycolysis/lipogenesis; ↓lipolysis; ↓gluconeogenesis; ↓ketogenesis. Without insulin: the metabolic opposite. (2) Hyperglycemia: glucose can't enter cells (except brain, liver, RBCs — insulin-independent GLUT transporters) → blood glucose rises. Liver: ↑gluconeogenesis (amino acids, glycerol → glucose) + ↑glycogenolysis → even higher blood glucose. Above renal threshold (~180 mg/dL) → osmotic diuresis (glucosuria) → dehydration, electrolyte loss (Na⁺, K⁺). (3) Ketogenesis: without insulin, hormone-sensitive lipase in adipose is fully active → ↑↑↑ lipolysis → massive free fatty acid release → liver β-oxidation → excess acetyl-CoA. Citric acid cycle overwhelmed (OAA depleted for gluconeogenesis) → acetyl-CoA → ketone bodies: acetoacetate, β-hydroxybutyrate (strong acids), acetone (volatile → fruity breath). (4) Metabolic acidosis: ketone accumulation → pH drops < 7.35 (normal: 7.35-7.45). Bicarbonate buffer exhausted. Compensatory: Kussmaul breathing (deep, rapid) → blow off CO₂ → attempt to raise pH. (5) Treatment: IV insulin (drives glucose into cells, stops ketogenesis), IV fluids (correct dehydration — average deficit 5-7L), electrolyte replacement (especially K⁺ — insulin drives K⁺ into cells → hypokalemia risk → cardiac arrhythmia). (6) Mortality: untreated DKA was uniformly fatal before insulin discovery (Banting & Best, 1921). With treatment, mortality ~1-5% (higher in developing countries).
Example: A dermatologist explains to a patient with severe sunburn that UV-B radiation caused the formation of thymine dimers in the DNA of her skin cells, and that her repair enzymes are working to fix the damage. The patient asks why this matters. Using your knowledge of everyday biochemistry, explain how UV damage, DNA repair, and antioxidants relate to skin cancer risk.
UV damage and skin cancer biochemistry: (1) UV-B (280-315 nm) directly absorbed by DNA bases → adjacent pyrimidines form covalent bonds: cyclobutane pyrimidine dimers (CPDs, most common: T=T, T=C) and 6-4 photoproducts. (2) Repair: nucleotide excision repair (NER) — a multi-enzyme system that recognizes bulky DNA distortions, excises ~24-32 nucleotides around the damage, and resynthesizes using the undamaged strand as template. In humans, ~10 proteins involved (XPA through XPG). Xeroderma pigmentosum (XP): autosomal recessive mutations in NER genes → inability to repair UV damage → 1,000× increased skin cancer risk → patients must avoid all sunlight. (3) Mutation signature: if unrepaired CPD is replicated → DNA polymerase inserts adenine opposite the dimer → C→T or CC→TT transitions. p53 tumor suppressor gene frequently mutated in skin cancers — specific 'UV signature' mutations confirm UV as causative. (4) UV-A (315-400 nm, penetrates deeper) → primarily indirect damage: generates ROS (singlet oxygen, superoxide) → oxidative DNA damage (8-oxoguanine), lipid peroxidation, protein oxidation. Antioxidants (vitamin C, E, polyphenols from green tea/berries) can neutralize these ROS. (5) Skin cancer statistics: most common cancer globally. Basal cell carcinoma (most common, rarely metastatic), squamous cell carcinoma, melanoma (deadliest — ~8,000 deaths/year in US). UV exposure is primary modifiable risk factor. (6) Sunscreen biochemistry: mineral/physical sunscreens (zinc oxide, titanium dioxide) reflect UV; chemical sunscreens (oxybenzone, avobenzone) absorb UV and dissipate as heat.

--- Lesson 6.6: Homeostasis (Quiz) ---

  Q1. How do vitamins function in the human body, and why are they essential despite being needed only in small amounts?
    [ ] Vitamins are the primary energy source for cellular metabolism — without them, cells cannot produce ATP or perform any biochemical reactions, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] Vitamins are only needed during childhood growth — adults produce all necessary vitamins endogenously through metabolic pathways in the liver, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] Vitamins primarily function as coenzymes or coenzyme precursors that assist enzymes in catalyzing biochemical reactions — they are essential because the body cannot synthesize most of them, yet they are required for metabolism, immune function, blood clotting, and many other processes — classified as fat-soluble or water-soluble
    [ ] Vitamins serve as structural building blocks for cell membranes and organelles — they are incorporated directly into the phospholipid bilayer to maintain membrane integrity, through the action of specific biochemical pathways and regulatory mechanisms that maintain cellular homeostasis and normal growth
    Explanation: Vitamins in biochemistry: (1) Coenzyme function: many B vitamins serve as coenzymes — NAD⁺ (from niacin/B3), FAD (from riboflavin/B2), coenzyme A (from pantothenic acid/B5), TPP (from thiamine/B1), PLP (from pyridoxine/B6). These coenzymes are essential participants in metabolic pathways (glycolysis, citric acid cycle, electron transport chain, amino acid metabolism). (2) Fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E, K): stored in liver and adipose tissue, can accumulate to toxic levels (hypervitaminosis). Vitamin A (retinol) — vision, gene expression; D (cholecalciferol) — calcium absorption, immune regulation (synthesized in skin via UV-B); E (tocopherol) — antioxidant protecting membrane lipids; K (phylloquinone) — blood clotting factors (carboxylation of glutamate residues), bone metabolism. (3) Water-soluble vitamins: B-complex (8 vitamins) + C (ascorbic acid). Excreted in urine → rarely toxic, but need regular intake. Vitamin C: collagen synthesis (hydroxyproline/hydroxylysine), antioxidant, iron absorption. Deficiency: scurvy (James Lind's citrus experiment, 1747 — one of the first clinical trials). (4) Deficiency diseases: beriberi (B1/thiamine), pellagra (B3/niacin — the 4 D's: dermatitis, diarrhea, dementia, death), pernicious anemia (B12 — intrinsic factor needed for absorption), rickets (vitamin D), night blindness (vitamin A). (5) Casimir Funk coined 'vitamine' (vital amine) in 1912.

  Q2. What is cholesterol's role in the body, and why is the distinction between LDL and HDL cholesterol important for health?
    [ ] Cholesterol is only obtained from dietary sources — the body cannot produce it, which is why high-cholesterol diets are the sole cause of heart disease, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] Cholesterol is purely harmful — it serves no biological purpose and should be completely eliminated from the diet for optimal health, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [*] Cholesterol is an essential lipid (sterol) that serves as a structural component of cell membranes (modulating fluidity), and is a precursor for steroid hormones, bile salts (fat digestion), and vitamin D, LDL ('bad' cholesterol) delivers cholesterol to tissues and can deposit in arterial walls causing atherosclerosis, while HDL ('good' cholesterol) transports cholesterol back to the liver for excretion
    [ ] Cholesterol is a type of protein that forms the structural framework of bones and cartilage — LDL transports it to bones while HDL removes it from joints, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Cholesterol biochemistry: (1) Structure: a 4-ring steroid with a hydroxyl group — amphipathic molecule. (2) Cell membrane role: cholesterol inserts between phospholipids, reducing fluidity at high temperatures (restricts movement) and preventing crystallization at low temperatures — acts as a 'fluidity buffer.' About 25-30% of mammalian membrane lipids. (3) Biosynthesis: liver produces ~800 mg/day (endogenous) via the mevalonate pathway. Key enzyme: HMG-CoA reductase (target of statin drugs — lovastatin discovered by Akira Endo, 1976). Dietary cholesterol absorption varies widely (~30-70%). (4) Lipoprotein transport: Cholesterol is hydrophobic → transported in blood via lipoproteins. LDL (Low-Density Lipoprotein) — delivers cholesterol to peripheral tissues; excess LDL → oxidized LDL → taken up by macrophages → foam cells → atherosclerotic plaques → heart attack/stroke. HDL (High-Density Lipoprotein) — removes excess cholesterol from tissues via reverse cholesterol transport to liver → protective. (5) Health guidelines: optimal LDL < 100 mg/dL; HDL > 60 mg/dL (protective); total cholesterol < 200 mg/dL. (6) Precursor roles: sex hormones (estrogen, progesterone, testosterone), adrenal cortical hormones (cortisol, aldosterone), bile salts (emulsify dietary fats), vitamin D (7-dehydrocholesterol in skin → UV-B → cholecalciferol).

  Q3. What causes lactose intolerance, and how does it illustrate the relationship between enzyme function and everyday digestion?
    [ ] Lactose intolerance is caused by an allergic immune response to milk proteins (casein and whey) — it involves IgE antibodies and can cause anaphylaxis, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [ ] Lactose intolerance occurs because the stomach's pH is too low to properly denature milk proteins — it can be cured by taking antacids before consuming dairy, incorporating data on the number of distinct taxa present and the statistical distribution of individual abundances among those taxa
    [ ] Lactose intolerance is caused by excess lactase production that breaks down too many sugars simultaneously, overwhelming the intestinal lining and causing inflammation, through the action of specific biochemical pathways and regulatory mechanisms that maintain cellular homeostasis and normal growth
    [*] Lactose intolerance results from insufficient production of lactase enzyme (β-galactosidase), which normally hydrolyzes lactose (a disaccharide) into glucose and galactose for absorption — without adequate lactase, undigested lactose passes to the colon where bacteria ferment it, producing gas (H₂, CO₂, CH₄), lactic acid, and short-chain fatty acids that cause bloating, cramping
    Explanation: Lactose intolerance biochemistry: (1) Lactose: β-1,4-glycosidic bond linking galactose to glucose. Requires lactase (β-galactosidase) in brush border of small intestinal epithelium for hydrolysis. (2) Lactase persistence vs. non-persistence: most mammals (and most humans globally) downregulate lactase after weaning (lactase non-persistence — the ancestral state). Lactase persistence (adult lactose tolerance) is a genetic trait: ~35% of global population. Most common in Northern Europeans (~95% lactase persistent) and some African pastoralist populations (Maasai, Fulani). (3) Genetics: caused by SNPs in regulatory region upstream of LCT gene (chr 2). In Europeans: -13910*T allele (C→T) — discovered 2002. Independent mutations arose in Africa (-13907*G, -13915*G, -14010*C). Classic example of convergent evolution driven by dairy farming culture (gene-culture coevolution). (4) Mechanism of symptoms: undigested lactose → osmotically draws water into intestinal lumen → colonic bacteria ferment lactose → H₂, CO₂, CH₄ (gas/bloating), lactic acid, short-chain fatty acids (acidic stool, cramping, diarrhea). (5) Diagnosis: hydrogen breath test (elevated H₂ after lactose ingestion). (6) Management: lactase enzyme supplements (Lactaid), lactose-free dairy products, or alternative calcium sources. (7) Important distinction: lactose intolerance ≠ milk allergy (which is an IgE-mediated immune response to casein or whey proteins — can cause anaphylaxis).

  Q4. How does fermentation work as a biochemical process, and why is it essential in food production?
    [*] Fermentation is anaerobic metabolism in which microorganisms (yeast, bacteria) convert sugars into useful byproducts without oxygen — alcohol fermentation by yeast (glucose → ethanol + CO₂) is used in bread (CO₂ makes dough rise), beer, and wine, while lactic acid fermentation by bacteria (glucose → lactic acid) is used to make yogurt, cheese, kimchi, and sauerkraut — preserving food by lowering pH and creating distinctive flavors
    [ ] Fermentation is a purely chemical (non-biological) oxidation process — no living organisms are involved; it occurs spontaneously when sugars are exposed to warm temperatures
    [ ] Fermentation requires extremely high temperatures (above 100°C) to break down sugar molecules, which is why all fermented foods must be cooked before consumption
    [ ] Fermentation is a form of aerobic respiration that requires oxygen to convert glucose into carbon dioxide and water — it's used in food production because oxygen improves flavor
    Explanation: Fermentation biochemistry: (1) Metabolic context: fermentation follows glycolysis (glucose → 2 pyruvate + 2 ATP + 2 NADH) but regenerates NAD⁺ without oxygen, allowing glycolysis to continue anaerobically. Far less efficient than aerobic respiration (2 ATP vs. 30-32 ATP per glucose). (2) Alcohol (ethanol) fermentation: pyruvate → acetaldehyde (via pyruvate decarboxylase, requires TPP/thiamine) + CO₂ → ethanol (via alcohol dehydrogenase, oxidizes NADH → NAD⁺). Organisms: Saccharomyces cerevisiae (baker's/brewer's yeast). Applications: bread (CO₂ = leavening agent, ethanol evaporates during baking), beer/wine (ethanol = desired product), biofuel production. (3) Lactic acid fermentation: pyruvate → lactate (via lactate dehydrogenase, oxidizes NADH → NAD⁺). Organisms: Lactobacillus, Streptococcus thermophilus. Applications: yogurt, cheese, kimchi, sauerkraut, kombucha. Also occurs in human muscle during intense exercise (oxygen debt → lactate accumulation → fatigue/soreness). (4) Other fermentations: acetic acid (vinegar — Acetobacter, actually aerobic), propionic acid (Swiss cheese holes — Propionibacterium). (5) Historical significance: Louis Pasteur (1857) demonstrated fermentation is caused by living organisms ('ferments'), disproving spontaneous generation. Buchner (1897) showed cell-free yeast extract could ferment — discovered enzymes (Nobel 1907). (6) Preservation: lactic acid lowers pH to ~3.5-4.5, inhibiting pathogenic bacteria — fermentation was one of humanity's earliest food preservation methods (~7000 BCE in China, ~6000 BCE in Mesopotamia).

  Q5. What are antioxidants, and how do they protect cells from damage caused by free radicals?
    [ ] Antioxidants are synthetic pharmaceutical compounds not found in natural foods — they were invented in the 1990s and have no natural biological equivalents
    [ ] Antioxidants work by removing all oxygen from cells, since oxygen itself is the sole cause of cellular damage — this is why anaerobic organisms never experience cell damage
    [*] Antioxidants are molecules (including vitamins C and E, glutathione, and carotenoids) that neutralize free radicals — unstable molecules with unpaired electrons that damage DNA, proteins, and cell membrane lipids through chain-reaction oxidation — by donating electrons to stabilize free radicals without becoming dangerously reactive themselves, thereby preventing oxidative stress linked to aging, cancer, cardiovascular disease, and neurodegenerative disorders
    [ ] Antioxidants are minerals that strengthen cell walls by forming cross-links between cellulose fibers — they prevent physical damage from environmental stress
    Explanation: Antioxidants and oxidative stress: (1) Free radicals: atoms or molecules with unpaired electrons — highly reactive. Reactive oxygen species (ROS): superoxide (O₂⁻•), hydroxyl radical (•OH), hydrogen peroxide (H₂O₂), singlet oxygen. Generated by: mitochondrial electron transport chain (1-3% of O₂ becomes superoxide normally), UV radiation, ionizing radiation, cigarette smoke, pollution, some drugs. (2) Damage mechanism: lipid peroxidation (chain reaction: free radical abstracts H from unsaturated fatty acid → lipid radical → reacts with O₂ → peroxyl radical → propagates to neighboring lipids → membrane damage). Also: DNA oxidation (8-oxoguanine → G→T transversions → mutations), protein oxidation (carbonylation → loss of function, aggregation). (3) Antioxidant defense: Enzymatic — superoxide dismutase (SOD: O₂⁻• → H₂O₂), catalase (H₂O₂ → H₂O + O₂), glutathione peroxidase (reduces H₂O₂ using GSH). Non-enzymatic — vitamin C (ascorbate, water-soluble, regenerates vitamin E), vitamin E (α-tocopherol, lipid-soluble, breaks lipid peroxidation chains), glutathione (GSH, most abundant intracellular antioxidant, tripeptide), carotenoids (β-carotene, lycopene), polyphenols (from fruits, vegetables, tea, coffee). (4) Oxidative stress: imbalance between ROS production and antioxidant defense → linked to aging (free radical theory of aging — Denham Harman, 1956), cancer, cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer's, Parkinson's.

  Q6. How do trans fats differ structurally from cis fats, and why are trans fats particularly harmful to cardiovascular health?
    [ ] Trans fats are synthetic polymers that cannot be broken down by any human enzyme — they accumulate permanently in the body and can never be eliminated, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [ ] Trans fats have more carbon atoms than cis fats, making them larger molecules that accumulate in blood vessels — they are harmful simply because of their molecular size, not their shape, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [*] Trans fats have hydrogen atoms on opposite sides of the carbon-carbon double bond, while cis fats have hydrogens on the same side (creating a kinked chain) — trans fats are harmful because they raise LDL cholesterol AND lower HDL cholesterol simultaneously, promote inflammation, and increase cardiovascular disease risk — most dietary trans fats come from industrial partial hydrogenation of vegetable oils
    [ ] Trans fats and cis fats are chemically identical — the only difference is that trans fats come from animal sources while cis fats come from plants, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Trans fats biochemistry: (1) Cis vs. trans isomerism: both have C=C double bonds, but differ in H arrangement. Cis: H atoms on same side → kinked chain → molecules don't pack tightly → liquid at room temperature (oils). Trans: H atoms on opposite sides → straight chain → pack tightly like saturated fats → solid at room temperature. (2) Sources: natural trans fats — small amounts in ruminant meat/dairy (vaccenic acid, conjugated linoleic acid — formed by bacterial enzymes in rumen). Industrial trans fats — partial hydrogenation of vegetable oils (invented by Paul Sabatier; commercialized as Crisco, 1911). Partial hydrogenation adds H atoms but can isomerize cis → trans. (3) Health effects: worst type of dietary fat for cardiovascular health. Raises LDL (bad) cholesterol + lowers HDL (good) → doubles the cardiovascular risk compared to equivalent calories from carbohydrates. Also promotes systemic inflammation (↑CRP, ↑IL-6), endothelial dysfunction, and insulin resistance. (4) WHO estimates trans fats cause ~500,000 cardiovascular deaths/year globally. (5) Regulation: FDA banned partially hydrogenated oils (PHOs) in US food supply (final compliance June 2018). WHO REPLACE initiative aims to eliminate industrial trans fats globally by 2023. Denmark was first country to ban (2003). (6) On food labels: 'partially hydrogenated oil' in ingredients = contains trans fats, even if label says '0g trans fat' (allowed if < 0.5g/serving).

  Q7. How do enzymes in laundry detergent work, and what biochemical principles make them effective at removing stains?
    [ ] Enzymes in detergent work by dissolving the fabric fibers around stains, releasing the embedded particles — this is why enzyme-containing detergents gradually weaken clothing over time
    [*] Enzymes in detergent are biological catalysts that break down specific stain molecules through hydrolysis — proteases digest protein stains (blood, grass, food), lipases break down fat/grease stains, amylases degrade starch stains, and cellulases smooth cotton fibers — they work at lower temperatures than traditional detergents, saving energy, and are biodegradable, making them more environmentally friendly
    [ ] Enzymes work only at very high temperatures (above 90°C) — they are inactive in cold water and must be activated by boiling
    [ ] Enzymes in detergent are actually not biological — they are synthetic chemical oxidizers that bleach stains by breaking all carbon bonds
    Explanation: Industrial enzyme biochemistry: (1) Principle: enzyme specificity (lock-and-key/induced fit model) allows targeting specific stain types. All work by hydrolysis — breaking bonds by adding water. (2) Types in detergent: Proteases (subtilisins — serine proteases from Bacillus) — break peptide bonds in protein stains (blood, egg, grass). Most common and most important detergent enzyme. Lipases — cleave ester bonds in triglycerides (grease, body oils, cooking fat) → glycerol + fatty acids (soluble/emulsifiable). Amylases (α-amylase) — hydrolyze α-1,4 glycosidic bonds in starch stains (pasta, potato, gravy). Cellulases — trim broken cellulose microfibrils from cotton → restores color brightness, reduces pilling. Mannanases — break down guar gum (thickener in foods, cosmetics). (3) Engineering: industrial enzymes are often from extremophilic organisms or engineered for alkaline pH (detergent pH 9-11) and moderate temperature (30-60°C). Protein engineering (directed evolution — Frances Arnold, Nobel 2018) and rational design improve stability, activity, and specificity. (4) Economic impact: global industrial enzyme market ~$7 billion/year. Detergent enzymes allow effective cleaning at 30-40°C instead of 60-90°C → significant energy savings (~40% less energy). (5) History: first enzyme detergent (Bio-40, containing subtilisin) introduced in 1960s. Initially caused allergic reactions in factory workers → reformulated as encapsulated granules.

  Q8. How do insulin and glucagon regulate blood glucose levels, and what happens when this regulation fails in diabetes?
    [ ] Blood glucose is regulated entirely by dietary intake — insulin and glucagon play no role in moment-to-moment regulation and only function during fasting periods, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] Insulin converts glucose into amino acids for muscle building, while glucagon converts amino acids back into glucose — diabetes occurs when this interconversion stops, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] Insulin and glucagon both raise blood sugar — they differ only in how quickly they act, with insulin being fast-acting and glucagon being slow-acting, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] Insulin and glucagon are antagonistic hormones that maintain blood glucose homeostasis — insulin lowers blood glucose by stimulating glucose uptake into cells and promoting glycogen synthesis, while glucagon raises it by stimulating glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis — in Type 1 diabetes, autoimmune destruction of β-cells eliminates insulin production, and in Type 2, cells become resistant to insulin's signal despite adequate production
    Explanation: Blood glucose regulation: (1) Normal fasting glucose: 70-100 mg/dL (3.9-5.6 mmol/L). (2) After eating (high glucose): β-cells of islets of Langerhans in pancreas release insulin → insulin binds insulin receptor (tyrosine kinase) on target cells → GLUT4 glucose transporters translocate to cell surface (muscle, adipose) → glucose uptake increases → liver: ↑glycogenesis (glycogen synthesis), ↑glycolysis, ↑lipogenesis. Blood glucose drops to normal. (3) During fasting (low glucose): α-cells release glucagon → liver: ↑glycogenolysis (glycogen breakdown → glucose), ↑gluconeogenesis (making glucose from amino acids, glycerol, lactate) → glucose released into blood. (4) Type 1 diabetes (T1D): autoimmune destruction of β-cells (T-cell mediated). Usually childhood onset. Complete insulin deficiency → uncontrolled hyperglycemia → ketoacidosis (fat breakdown → ketones → metabolic acidosis). ~5-10% of diabetes cases. Treatment: exogenous insulin (Banting & Best, 1921 — Nobel 1923). (5) Type 2 diabetes (T2D): insulin resistance (cells respond poorly to insulin) + progressive β-cell dysfunction. ~90% of cases. Associated with obesity, physical inactivity, genetic predisposition. Initially hyperinsulinemia (β-cells compensate) → eventually β-cell exhaustion. Treatment: lifestyle modification, metformin (decreases hepatic glucose production), insulin in later stages. (6) Global epidemic: ~537 million adults with diabetes (2021 IDF). Complications: retinopathy, nephropathy, neuropathy, cardiovascular disease.

  Q9. What is bioluminescence, and how do the biochemical reactions that produce biological light illustrate enzyme catalysis?
    [ ] Bioluminescence occurs only in deep-sea organisms and requires extremely high pressure to function — fireflies and other terrestrial organisms that appear to glow are actually using fluorescence, not bioluminescence
    [ ] Bioluminescence is the reflection of sunlight stored in specialized cells during the day and released at night — organisms like fireflies act like biological batteries that slowly discharge light energy
    [ ] Bioluminescence is produced by tiny electrical sparks between nerve cells in light-producing organs — it is essentially biological electricity converted to light, similar to a light bulb
    [*] Bioluminescence is the production of light through enzyme-catalyzed chemical reactions — the enzyme luciferase catalyzes the oxidation of a substrate called luciferin, using ATP and molecular oxygen to produce oxyluciferin and a photon of visible light — this illustrates enzyme catalysis because luciferase lowers the activation energy for this specific oxidation, making it efficient enough to produce 'cold light' (nearly 100% energy converted to light, not heat)
    Explanation: Bioluminescence biochemistry: (1) General reaction: Luciferin + O₂ → (luciferase catalyst, often ATP-dependent) → Oxyluciferin + Light. The terms luciferin/luciferase (from Latin 'lucifer' = light-bearer) are generic — different organisms evolved different luciferins independently. (2) Firefly (Photinus pyralis): D-luciferin + ATP + O₂ → (firefly luciferase) → oxyluciferin + AMP + PPi + CO₂ + light (λ 560 nm, yellow-green). Quantum yield ~0.88 (88% of reactions produce a photon — among the most efficient chemical reactions known). Used for mating signals. (3) Marine bioluminescence: coelenterazine is the most common marine luciferin (jellyfish Aequorea victoria uses it — source of GFP, Green Fluorescent Protein: Shimomura discovered aequorin, Chalfie & Tsien engineered GFP — Nobel 2008). Deep-sea anglerfish use symbiotic bioluminescent bacteria (Photobacterium). ~76% of deep-sea organisms are bioluminescent. (4) Applications: GFP revolutionized cell biology (gene expression reporter, protein localization tracking). ATP-dependent luciferase used as biosensor for contamination detection (ATP = indicator of living cells). Bioluminescence imaging in cancer research (tumor monitoring in mice). (5) Evolution: bioluminescence evolved independently at least 40-50 times across diverse taxa — bacteria, dinoflagellates, fungi, jellyfish, corals, insects, fish, squid.

  Q10. How is PCR (Polymerase Chain Reaction) used in medicine and forensics, and what biochemical principles does it rely on?
    [ ] PCR counts the total number of chromosomes in a cell sample — it is used to diagnose chromosomal disorders like Down syndrome but cannot identify specific DNA sequences
    [ ] PCR merges DNA fragments from different organisms to create hybrid molecules — this is why it is mainly used in genetic engineering rather than forensics or medicine
    [ ] PCR uses chemical dyes to stain specific DNA sequences visible to the naked eye — it is useful because different genes absorb different wavelengths of light, allowing direct visual identification
    [*] PCR exponentially amplifies specific DNA sequences by exploiting three key biochemical principles: DNA denaturation (95°C separates strands by breaking hydrogen bonds), primer annealing (50-65°C allows short DNA primers to bind flanking sequences through complementary base pairing), and extension (72°C is optimal for Taq DNA polymerase to synthesize new strands) — this enables detection of minute DNA quantities in forensic evidence, diagnosis of infectious diseases, genetic testing, and paternity testing
    Explanation: PCR in everyday applications: (1) Invented by Kary Mullis, 1983 (Nobel 1993). Described as 'making mountains out of molehills' for DNA. (2) Key requirements: template DNA, 2 primers (~18-25 nt, flanking target region), dNTPs, thermostable DNA polymerase (Taq from Thermus aquaticus — revolutionized the process; also Pfu from Pyrococcus furiosus — higher fidelity), buffer with Mg²⁺. (3) Cycle: Denaturation (94-95°C, 30s) → Annealing (50-65°C, 30s — primer Tm dependent) → Extension (72°C, time depends on amplicon length). 25-35 cycles → 2²⁵-2³⁵ copies (~33 million to 34 billion). (4) Medical applications: infectious disease diagnosis (COVID-19 RT-qPCR testing — gold standard; detects viral RNA via reverse transcription + PCR), genetic testing (BRCA1/2 mutations, cystic fibrosis carrier screening), prenatal diagnosis, HLA typing for organ transplant matching, monitoring viral load (HIV). (5) Forensic applications: STR profiling from crime scene DNA (blood, saliva, hair follicles, skin cells — as little as 100 pg DNA), paternity testing, identifying disaster victims, cold case investigations. (6) Variants: RT-PCR (reverse transcription — for RNA), qPCR (quantitative/real-time — measures amplification in real time for quantification), digital PCR (absolute quantification), multiplex PCR (multiple targets simultaneously). (7) Historical impact: PCR transformed molecular biology, enabling the Human Genome Project, metagenomics, ancient DNA research, and personalized medicine.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes dehydration synthesis (condensation reaction)?
    [ ] The evaporation of water from a cell during heat stress, causing proteins to denature and lose their biological function, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] A reaction that breaks a polymer into its monomers by adding a water molecule across the bond connecting them, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] The process by which water molecules are split during photosynthesis to release oxygen gas, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [*] A chemical reaction that joins two monomers by removing a water molecule to form a covalent bond — this is the universal mechanism by which cells build all biological polymers: polysaccharides (glycosidic bonds), proteins (peptide bonds), nucleic acids (phosphodiester bonds)
    Explanation: Dehydration synthesis: (1) Mechanism: monomer-OH + H-monomer → monomer-O-monomer + H₂O. A hydroxyl group (-OH) from one monomer combines with a hydrogen (-H) from another, releasing water and forming a covalent bond. Requires energy input (endergonic reaction) — in cells, often coupled to ATP hydrolysis or driven by enzyme catalysis. (2) Specific bond types formed: peptide bonds (between amino acids → proteins; catalyzed by ribosomes), glycosidic bonds (between monosaccharides → polysaccharides; α-1,4 in starch/glycogen, β-1,4 in cellulose), phosphodiester bonds (between nucleotides → DNA/RNA; catalyzed by polymerases), ester bonds (between glycerol and fatty acids → triglycerides/phospholipids). (3) Energy requirement: peptide bond formation on ribosome uses ~4 ATP equivalents per amino acid. Glycosidic bond formation uses activated sugar precursors (e.g., UDP-glucose). (4) Net result: monomers → polymer + n-1 water molecules (where n = number of monomers added). (5) Reversibility: dehydration synthesis is reversed by hydrolysis (adding water back to break bonds). These two reactions are the fundamental building/breaking mechanisms in all of biochemistry. (6) Also called 'condensation reaction' in chemistry — the same mechanism is used in industrial polymerization (nylon, polyester synthetic polymers).

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes hydrolysis?
    [ ] A reaction that builds polymers by removing water molecules from between monomers to form covalent bonds, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] The process by which water molecules move across a semipermeable membrane from low solute to high solute concentration, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] The splitting of water by electrical current in a laboratory procedure used to generate hydrogen and oxygen gas, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] A chemical reaction that breaks a covalent bond in a polymer by adding a water molecule across the bond — the water's hydrogen (H) attaches to one fragment and the hydroxyl group (OH) to the other, releasing individual monomers — this is how cells digest food and recycle cellular components
    Explanation: Hydrolysis (Greek: hydro = water, lysis = breaking): (1) Mechanism: polymer + H₂O → (enzyme) → monomers. Water is added across the covalent bond, regenerating the -OH and -H groups that were removed during dehydration synthesis. Exergonic reaction (releases energy). (2) Digestive hydrolysis: Proteins → amino acids (pepsin in stomach pH 2, trypsin/chymotrypsin in small intestine pH 8). Starch → maltose → glucose (salivary amylase, pancreatic amylase, maltase). Fats → glycerol + fatty acids (pancreatic lipase + bile salts). DNA/RNA → nucleotides (nucleases, phosphodiesterases). (3) Intracellular hydrolysis: lysosomes (pH ~4.5-5, contain 50+ acid hydrolases) — digest engulfed materials, worn-out organelles (autophagy), extracellular debris. Proteasomes — tagged (ubiquitinated) proteins → peptides. (4) ATP hydrolysis: ATP + H₂O → ADP + Pi (ΔG = -30.5 kJ/mol) — the primary energy-coupling reaction powering endergonic cellular processes (muscle contraction, active transport, biosynthesis). (5) Thermodynamics: hydrolysis is energetically favorable (negative ΔG), but the reverse (dehydration synthesis) is unfavorable — cells must input energy (ATP, activated intermediates) to build polymers. (6) Industrial applications: enzymatic hydrolysis in biofuel production (cellulases break cellulose → glucose → fermented to ethanol), detergent enzymes (proteases, lipases), cheese production (rennin/chymosin hydrolyzes κ-casein).

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes an amino acid?
    [ ] A nucleotide building block composed of a sugar, phosphate group, and nitrogenous base used to build DNA and RNA polymers, as determined through molecular analysis of DNA sequence variation and allele frequency distributions across sampled populations
    [ ] A simple sugar (monosaccharide) containing both an amine group and an acidic hydroxyl group that is used for quick energy in metabolism, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range
    [*] An organic molecule that serves as the monomer of proteins — each consisting of a central carbon (α-carbon) bonded to an amino group (-NH₂), a carboxyl group (-COOH), a hydrogen atom
    [ ] A fatty acid that has been modified by the addition of a nitrogen-containing group, making it soluble in both water and lipids, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological
    Explanation: Amino acids — protein building blocks: (1) General structure: H₂N-CHR-COOH (at physiological pH: ⁺H₃N-CHR-COO⁻, zwitterion). The R-group (side chain) varies among the 20 standard amino acids and determines: charge (acidic: Asp, Glu; basic: Lys, Arg, His), polarity (polar: Ser, Thr, Asn, Gln; nonpolar: Ala, Val, Leu, Ile, Phe, Trp, Met, Pro), size, and special properties (Cys: disulfide bonds; Pro: cyclic → kinks in chain; Gly: smallest, fits in tight spaces). (2) Chirality: all amino acids except glycine have a chiral α-carbon. Biological amino acids are L-form (left-handed). D-amino acids found in bacterial cell walls and some antibiotics. (3) Essential vs. non-essential: 9 essential amino acids cannot be synthesized by humans (must come from diet): His, Ile, Leu, Lys, Met, Phe, Thr, Trp, Val. Mnemonic: 'PVT TIM HALL.' (4) Peptide bond: formed by dehydration synthesis between -COOH of one amino acid and -NH₂ of the next. Partial double bond character → planar → restricted rotation (Pauling). (5) Protein diversity: 20²⁰ possible sequences for a 20-amino-acid protein = 10²⁶ combinations. Average human protein ~375 amino acids. Human proteome: ~20,000 protein-coding genes, but 80,000-400,000 distinct proteins (alternative splicing, post-translational modifications). (6) Beyond proteins: amino acid derivatives — neurotransmitters (tryptophan → serotonin → melatonin; tyrosine → dopamine → norepinephrine → epinephrine), thyroid hormones (tyrosine + iodine), histamine (histidine decarboxylation).

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes a saturated fat?
    [ ] A fat molecule that contains one or more carbon-carbon double bonds in its fatty acid chains, creating kinks that prevent tight packing, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] A fat that has been artificially saturated with nitrogen gas to increase its shelf life and prevent spoilage in processed foods, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] A carbohydrate molecule that is completely hydrated with water molecules, giving it a gel-like consistency used for energy storage, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] A lipid whose fatty acid chains contain no carbon-carbon double bonds — every carbon atom is bonded to the maximum number of hydrogen atoms — this allows the straight hydrocarbon chains to pack tightly together, making saturated fats solid at room temperature, and they are found predominantly in animal products and tropical oils
    Explanation: Saturated fats: (1) Structure: fatty acid chains with only single bonds between carbon atoms (C-C). General formula of fatty acid: CH₃(CH₂)ₙCOOH. Common saturated fatty acids: lauric acid (C12, coconut oil), myristic (C14), palmitic (C16, most common in the body), stearic (C18, beef tallow). (2) Physical properties: straight chains → tight van der Waals packing → high melting point → solid at room temperature and body temperature. Longer chain = higher melting point. (3) Sources: animal products (butter ~66% saturated, lard ~40%, cheese, whole milk, fatty meat), tropical plant oils (coconut oil ~82% saturated — highest of any common oil, palm oil ~49%). (4) Health effects: dietary saturated fat raises blood LDL cholesterol (mechanism: suppresses LDL receptor expression on liver cells → less LDL clearance from blood). However, the degree of risk is debated and varies by specific fatty acid — stearic acid (C18:0) appears less atherogenic than palmitic (C16:0). Current dietary guidelines recommend limiting saturated fat to <10% of total calories. (5) Comparison: saturated fats are more chemically stable than unsaturated (no C=C double bonds to oxidize) → longer shelf life, less rancidity. This is why the food industry historically preferred them or used hydrogenation to convert unsaturated → saturated. (6) Biological roles: structural component of cell membranes (mixed with unsaturated and cholesterol), energy storage (9 kcal/g, most energy-dense macronutrient), insulation, organ protection.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes an unsaturated fat?
    [ ] A type of protein that has not been fully assembled on the ribosome, leaving open binding sites for additional amino acids, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] A lipid whose fatty acid chains contain no double bonds and are fully bonded to hydrogen atoms, making them solid at room temperature, shaped by regional patterns in temperature, precipitation, prevailing winds, and the interactions between ocean and atmosphere
    [ ] A fat that has been partially depleted of its hydrogen atoms through a chemical process that makes it suitable for use as a biofuel, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] A lipid whose fatty acid chains contain one or more carbon-carbon double bonds — monounsaturated fats have one double bond, polyunsaturated fats have two or more — the double bonds create kinks in the chain preventing tight packing, so unsaturated fats are liquid at room temperature and are generally considered heart-healthy
    Explanation: Unsaturated fats: (1) Structure: one or more C=C double bonds in fatty acid chain → introduces a ~30° kink (cis configuration). Monounsaturated fatty acids (MUFA): one double bond — oleic acid (C18:1 Δ9, cis; major component of olive oil ~75%, avocado, almonds). Polyunsaturated fatty acids (PUFA): two or more double bonds — linoleic acid (C18:2 ω-6), α-linolenic acid (C18:3 ω-3), EPA (C20:5 ω-3), DHA (C22:6 ω-3). (2) Essential fatty acids: linoleic (ω-6) and α-linolenic (ω-3) cannot be synthesized by humans (lack Δ12 and Δ15 desaturases) → must come from diet. (3) Omega numbering: ω-3 = first double bond at 3rd carbon from methyl (omega) end. ω-6 = first double bond at 6th carbon. This determines which eicosanoids (signaling molecules) are produced: ω-6 → pro-inflammatory prostaglandins (from arachidonic acid); ω-3 → anti-inflammatory resolvins, protectins (from EPA/DHA). (4) Health benefits: MUFA and PUFA reduce LDL cholesterol and cardiovascular risk. Mediterranean diet (high olive oil) associated with ~30% reduction in heart attacks (PREDIMED trial, 2013). Omega-3 (fish oil): reduces triglycerides, anti-inflammatory, neuroprotective (DHA = ~40% of brain membrane fatty acids). (5) Physical state: kinks prevent close packing → lower melting point → liquid at room temperature ('oils'). More double bonds = lower melting point (olive oil solid below ~6°C; fish oil remains liquid even at 0°C). (6) Vulnerability: C=C double bonds are susceptible to oxidation (rancidity) — why unsaturated oils should be stored away from heat, light, and oxygen.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes a coenzyme (vitamin cofactor)?
    [ ] A large protein subunit that replaces the enzyme's active site when the enzyme is damaged by heat or pH changes, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [*] A small organic molecule that binds to an enzyme and is required for its catalytic activity — coenzymes function as temporary carriers of specific chemical groups or electrons between reactions, and include NAD⁺ (from niacin/B3), FAD (from riboflavin/B2), coenzyme A (from pantothenic acid/B5)
    [ ] A type of enzyme that functions independently without any substrate or active site — coenzymes catalyze reactions on their own, as governed by the molecular machinery of gene expression, protein synthesis, and intracellular transport within living cells overall
    [ ] A mineral ion such as iron or zinc that forms the structural backbone of all enzyme molecules, through the action of specific biochemical pathways and regulatory mechanisms that maintain cellular homeostasis and normal growth
    Explanation: Coenzymes and cofactors: (1) Definitions: cofactor = any non-protein chemical required for enzyme activity. Inorganic cofactors: metal ions (Zn²⁺ in carbonic anhydrase, Fe²⁺/Fe³⁺ in cytochromes, Mg²⁺ in DNA polymerase, Cu²⁺ in cytochrome c oxidase). Organic cofactors = coenzymes (loosely bound, dissociate after reaction) or prosthetic groups (tightly/permanently bound — e.g., heme in hemoglobin). (2) Holoenzyme = apoenzyme (protein) + cofactor (active). Apoenzyme alone = inactive. (3) Vitamin-derived coenzymes: NAD⁺/NADH (niacin/B3 → electron carrier in redox reactions, glycolysis, citric acid cycle), FAD/FADH₂ (riboflavin/B2 → electron carrier, succinate dehydrogenase), Coenzyme A (pantothenic acid/B5 → acyl group carrier, acetyl-CoA in citric acid cycle), TPP thiamine pyrophosphate (thiamine/B1 → decarboxylation reactions, pyruvate dehydrogenase), PLP pyridoxal phosphate (pyridoxine/B6 → amino acid metabolism, transamination), THF tetrahydrofolate (folate/B9 → one-carbon transfers, DNA synthesis), cobalamin (B12 → methyl group transfers, homocysteine → methionine). (4) Why vitamins are essential: humans lack biosynthetic pathways for most coenzyme precursors → must obtain from diet. (5) Vitamin deficiency → coenzyme deficiency → impaired enzyme function → disease (e.g., B1 deficiency → impaired pyruvate dehydrogenase → beriberi; B3 deficiency → impaired NAD⁺ → pellagra).

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes cholesterol?
    [ ] A water-soluble carbohydrate polymer that circulates in the bloodstream to deliver glucose to tissues during periods of fasting, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] A type of saturated fatty acid found exclusively in plant-based oils that serves as the primary energy storage molecule in the human body, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] A nucleic acid derivative that functions as a secondary messenger in cellular signaling pathways related to immune defense, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] A waxy sterol lipid (4-ring steroid structure) that is an essential component of animal cell membranes and serves as the precursor for synthesis of steroid hormones, bile salts (fat digestion), and vitamin D — transported in blood by lipoproteins (LDL and HDL), with excess LDL deposition in arteries contributing to atherosclerosis
    Explanation: Cholesterol — definition and significance: (1) Structure: C₂₇H₄₆O — a sterol (steroid alcohol) with 4 fused carbon rings (A, B, C, D), a hydrocarbon tail, and a hydroxyl (-OH) group. Amphipathic: -OH group interacts with aqueous environment, rest is hydrophobic. (2) Membrane function: inserts between phospholipids with -OH near polar head groups, rigid ring structure restricts fatty acid chain movement → at physiological temperature: decreases membrane fluidity (restricts movement); at low temperatures: prevents solidification (disrupts tight packing) → 'fluidity buffer'. Especially enriched in lipid rafts (signaling platforms). (3) Biosynthesis: primarily in liver (endoplasmic reticulum) via the mevalonate pathway (~30 enzymatic steps). Key regulatory enzyme: HMG-CoA reductase (target of statin drugs — most prescribed class of drugs globally). (4) Transport: lipoproteins — chylomicrons (dietary fat/cholesterol from gut), VLDL (liver → peripheral tissues), LDL (derived from VLDL, delivers cholesterol to tissues — when oxidized, taken up by macrophages → foam cells → atherosclerotic plaques), HDL (reverse cholesterol transport: periphery → liver). (5) Derivatives: bile acids (cholesterol → cholic acid, chenodeoxycholic acid → conjugated bile salts for fat emulsification), steroid hormones (progesterone → cortisol, aldosterone, testosterone, estradiol), vitamin D3 (7-dehydrocholesterol → UV-B in skin → cholecalciferol).

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes fermentation?
    [ ] The photosynthetic process by which plants convert carbon dioxide into glucose using sunlight energy captured by chlorophyll, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [ ] The chemical decomposition of organic matter by heat alone, without any biological organisms involved in the process, based on comprehensive taxonomic inventories and ecological census data collected across geographically distinct survey regions
    [ ] The complete aerobic oxidation of glucose to carbon dioxide and water in the presence of oxygen, producing 30-32 ATP per glucose molecule, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    [*] An anaerobic metabolic process in which microorganisms (or cells) convert sugars into energy (ATP) without oxygen — regenerating NAD⁺ from NADH to sustain glycolysis — producing characteristic byproducts: ethanol and CO₂ in alcohol fermentation or lactic acid in lactic acid fermentation
    Explanation: Fermentation — definition and applications: (1) Metabolic context: glycolysis (glucose → 2 pyruvate + 2 ATP + 2 NADH) occurs in all fermentation. The problem: NAD⁺ is limited → must be regenerated for glycolysis to continue. Aerobic path: NADH → electron transport chain → NAD⁺ (requires O₂). Anaerobic path (fermentation): pyruvate accepts electrons from NADH → NAD⁺ regenerated. (2) Alcohol fermentation: pyruvate → acetaldehyde + CO₂ (pyruvate decarboxylase, requires TPP) → ethanol (alcohol dehydrogenase, NADH → NAD⁺). Net: glucose → 2 ethanol + 2 CO₂ + 2 ATP. Organism: Saccharomyces cerevisiae. (3) Lactic acid fermentation: pyruvate → lactate (lactate dehydrogenase, NADH → NAD⁺). Net: glucose → 2 lactate + 2 ATP. Organisms: Lactobacillus, Streptococcus thermophilus. Also in human skeletal muscle during oxygen deficit. (4) Only 2 ATP per glucose (vs. 30-32 for aerobic respiration) — fermentation is ~5.5% as efficient. (5) Uses: bread (CO₂ leavening), beer/wine/spirits (ethanol production — Louis Pasteur was first to link fermentation to yeast, 1857), yogurt/cheese/pickles/kimchi/sauerkraut (lactic acid → flavor + preservation by lowering pH to ~4), industrial ethanol/biofuel production. (6) Buchner (1897) discovered cell-free fermentation (yeast extract) → proved enzymes, not living cells per se, catalyze fermentation → Nobel 1907. Foundation of biochemistry as a discipline.

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes an antioxidant?
    [ ] A mineral supplement that strengthens bones by increasing calcium deposition and preventing osteoporosis in aging adults, a concept that has been extensively studied and documented in ecological and evolutionary research across diverse biological systems
    [*] A molecule that protects cells from oxidative damage by neutralizing free radicals — donating electrons to stabilize reactive oxygen species (ROS) without becoming dangerously reactive itself — includes dietary antioxidants and endogenous antioxidant enzymes that defend against DNA mutations, lipid peroxidation
    [ ] A type of preservative added to processed foods that works by removing all oxygen from the packaging to prevent bacterial growth, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] An antibiotic compound produced by fungi that kills bacteria by oxidizing their cell membranes, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Antioxidant — definition and mechanisms: (1) Free radicals and ROS: molecules with unpaired electrons — extremely reactive. Major ROS: superoxide (O₂⁻•), hydroxyl radical (•OH, most reactive), hydrogen peroxide (H₂O₂), singlet oxygen (¹O₂). Sources: mitochondrial ETC (1-3% of O₂ 'leaks' as superoxide), NADPH oxidase (immune cells — respiratory burst), UV radiation, cigarette smoke, pollution, some drugs (doxorubicin). (2) Damage: lipid peroxidation (chain reaction: one radical → thousands of oxidized membrane lipids), DNA oxidation (8-oxoguanine → G→T transversion mutations; ~10,000 oxidative DNA lesions/cell/day), protein carbonylation (irreversible, targets for proteasomal degradation). (3) Enzymatic antioxidants: SOD (superoxide dismutase: O₂⁻• → H₂O₂; 3 isoforms: Cu/Zn-SOD cytoplasmic, Mn-SOD mitochondrial, EC-SOD extracellular), catalase (2 H₂O₂ → 2 H₂O + O₂; peroxisomes), glutathione peroxidase (H₂O₂ + 2 GSH → 2 H₂O + GSSG; selenium-dependent). (4) Non-enzymatic: vitamin C (ascorbate — water-soluble, regenerates vitamin E, scavenges aqueous ROS), vitamin E (α-tocopherol — lipid-soluble, chain-breaking antioxidant in membranes), glutathione (GSH — most abundant intracellular thiol antioxidant, ~5 mM), carotenoids (β-carotene, lycopene — singlet oxygen quenchers), polyphenols (flavonoids in tea/berries/cocoa, resveratrol in red wine). (5) Oxidative stress theory of aging (Denham Harman, 1956): cumulative oxidative damage → aging and age-related diseases. Supported but not sole mechanism.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes an enzyme (in the context of everyday biochemistry)?
    [ ] A nucleotide sequence in DNA that codes for a specific trait and is passed from parent to offspring during reproduction, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [ ] A type of white blood cell that identifies and destroys foreign pathogens through the adaptive immune response, as governed by the molecular machinery of gene expression, protein synthesis, and intracellular transport within living cells overall
    [*] A biological catalyst — typically a protein — that accelerates specific biochemical reactions by lowering the activation energy without being consumed in the process — enzymes are used in everyday applications including digestion, food production, laundry detergents, medical diagnostics, and industrial biotechnology
    [ ] A type of lipid that stores energy in adipose tissue and is released during periods of fasting to maintain blood glucose levels, through the action of specific biochemical pathways and regulatory mechanisms that maintain cellular homeostasis and normal growth
    Explanation: Enzymes in everyday life: (1) Fundamentals: enzymes lower activation energy (Ea) by 10⁶-10¹² fold. Each enzyme is specific to its substrate(s) — lock-and-key model (Emil Fischer, 1894) and induced-fit model (Daniel Koshland, 1958). Not consumed → catalytic amounts needed. (2) Everyday digestion: salivary amylase (starch → maltose, pH 6.8), pepsin (proteins → peptides, pH 2), pancreatic lipase + bile salts (triglycerides → glycerol + fatty acids), trypsin/chymotrypsin (proteins → peptides, pH 8), lactase (lactose → glucose + galactose). (3) Food industry: rennet/chymosin (milk → cheese — cleaves κ-casein), pectinase (fruit juice clarification), glucose isomerase (glucose → fructose for HFCS production — largest industrial enzyme use by volume), papain/bromelain (meat tenderizers — protease from papaya/pineapple). (4) Laundry: subtilisin (protease from Bacillus), lipase, amylase, cellulase — work at 30-40°C, alkaline pH. (5) Medical: diagnostic enzymes as biomarkers (troponin/CK-MB for heart attack, ALT/AST for liver damage), therapeutic enzymes (tissue plasminogen activator/tPA for blood clots, asparaginase for leukemia), enzyme replacement therapy (imiglucerase for Gaucher disease). (6) Industrial biotech: cellulases for biofuel (cellulose → glucose → ethanol), restriction enzymes + ligase for genetic engineering, Taq polymerase for PCR, CRISPR-Cas9 for gene editing. (7) Global enzyme market: ~$7 billion/year. Protein engineering (directed evolution — Frances Arnold, Nobel 2018) designs enzymes for specific industrial conditions.

  Q21. A nutrition label on a packaged cookie states: 'Calories: 200, Total Fat: 10g (Saturated 3g, Trans 0g), Total Carbohydrates: 25g (Sugars 15g), Protein: 2g.' However, the ingredient list includes 'partially hydrogenated soybean oil.' A biochemistry student realizes this is misleading. Why might the label say '0g trans fat' while still containing trans fats, and how should the student evaluate this product?
    [ ] The label is accurate because partial hydrogenation only creates cis fats, not trans fats — the process adds hydrogen atoms to the same side of the double bond
    [*] FDA labeling rules allow products to list '0g trans fat' if a serving contains less than 0.5g — since 'partially hydrogenated oil' is in the ingredients, the product does contain trans fats (likely 0.1-0.49g per serving) — consuming multiple servings accumulates meaningful amounts, and the student should read ingredient lists (not just nutrition facts) to identify hidden trans fats, which raise LDL, lower HDL, and increase cardiovascular risk
    [ ] If the label says 0g trans fat, the product is guaranteed to be completely free of trans fats — partially hydrogenated oil must refer to a fully hydrogenated and therefore safe ingredient
    [ ] Trans fats are only harmful in quantities above 5g per serving — anything below that threshold has no measurable effect on cholesterol levels or cardiovascular health
    Explanation: Hidden trans fats in food labeling: (1) FDA labeling loophole (pre-2018 ban, still relevant for some products): manufacturers could round down to 0g if serving contained < 0.5g trans fat. Strategy: reduce serving size to get trans fat below 0.5g → label '0g trans fat.' (2) The giveaway: 'partially hydrogenated oil' in ingredients = contains trans fats. Partial hydrogenation adds some (not all) H atoms to unsaturated fatty acids, and the process inevitably isomerizes some cis double bonds to trans configuration. Fully hydrogenated oils (all double bonds eliminated → saturated) contain no trans fats. (3) Cumulative exposure: 0.49g × 4 servings = ~2g trans fat. WHO recommends < 1% of total calories from trans fat (~2.2g for 2000 kcal diet). Even small amounts increase cardiovascular risk. (4) FDA banned partially hydrogenated oils (PHOs) from US food supply (final compliance June 2018) — but some products with extended shelf lives may still contain them, and exemptions/enforcement gaps exist. (5) Global progress: Denmark first country to effectively ban (2003, ≤ 2% of total fat). WHO REPLACE initiative targets global elimination. As of 2023, ~60 countries have implemented regulations. (6) Lesson: ingredient lists > nutrition facts labels for identifying problematic substances.

  Q22. A marathon runner collapses at mile 20 — the infamous 'hitting the wall.' Medical staff find she is hypoglycemic (blood glucose 52 mg/dL) and barely conscious. She had eaten only a large pasta dinner the night before and skipped breakfast. Using your knowledge of biochemistry, explain what happened metabolically and how dehydration synthesis and hydrolysis relate to energy storage and release during exercise.
    [ ] She collapsed because the lactic acid from the first 20 miles completely destroyed her muscle fibers — once muscles are acidified, they can never contract again until the lactate is surgically removed
    [ ] She collapsed because her body ran out of protein — the protein from the pasta dinner was completely used up by mile 10, and without protein, muscles cannot produce force
    [*] During the pasta dinner, dehydration synthesis reactions converted glucose monomers into glycogen polymers stored in liver (~100g) and muscles (~400g) — during the marathon, hydrolysis reactions broke glycogen back into glucose for ATP production — by mile 20 (~2 hours at marathon pace), glycogen stores were depleted, forcing metabolism to shift to slower fat oxidation, causing the dramatic drop in blood glucose and ATP production rate (the 'wall') — her skipped breakfast meant liver glycogen was already partially depleted at the start
    [ ] The collapse was caused by dehydration only — blood glucose is irrelevant during exercise because muscles use a completely different energy source (creatine phosphate) for the entire duration of a marathon
    Explanation: Marathon metabolism and the polymer-monomer connection: (1) Carb-loading: the pasta dinner provided glucose → dehydration synthesis stored it as glycogen (α-1,4 and α-1,6 glycosidic bonds). Muscle glycogen (~400g, ~1,600 kcal) for local use. Liver glycogen (~100g, ~400 kcal) for blood glucose maintenance. Total: ~2,000 kcal from glycogen. (2) Marathon energy demand: ~100 kcal/mile × 26.2 miles = ~2,620 kcal. At marathon pace, fuel is ~50-70% carbohydrate, ~30-50% fat (ratio depends on intensity and training). (3) Progressive fuel depletion: miles 1-10: glycogen + fat → plenty of ATP. Miles 10-18: glycogen declining, fat oxidation increasing. Mile ~20: glycogen essentially depleted ('the wall'). Fat oxidation cannot produce ATP fast enough to maintain pace (fat yields more total ATP per gram but at slower rate due to β-oxidation complexity). (4) Blood glucose crash: liver glycogen (maintained blood glucose) runs out → hypoglycemia → brain relies almost exclusively on glucose → confusion, dizziness, collapse. (5) Skipped breakfast: liver glycogen already depleted overnight (used to maintain blood glucose during sleep) → started race with ~50-60g liver glycogen instead of ~100g. (6) Hydrolysis is the key catabolic reaction: glycogen phosphorylase + debranching enzyme → glucose-1-phosphate → glucose-6-phosphate → glycolysis → pyruvate → mitochondria. (7) Prevention: sports nutrition recommends carb-loading 48-72 hours before, breakfast 3-4 hours before race, mid-race fueling (gels/drinks every 30-45 min to maintain blood glucose).

  Q23. A patient visits a gastroenterologist complaining of severe abdominal pain and fatty, foul-smelling stools (steatorrhea) after meals. Tests reveal that the patient's gallbladder was removed (cholecystectomy) two months ago, and she is not following dietary recommendations. How does the absence of concentrated bile affect fat digestion, and which biochemical processes are impaired?
    [*] Bile is produced by the liver from cholesterol — the gallbladder concentrates and stores bile between meals — without it, bile still enters the small intestine but in a constant dilute trickle rather than a concentrated bolus after a fatty meal — this results in inadequate emulsification of large fat globules into small micelles, reducing the surface area for pancreatic lipase to catalyze hydrolysis of triglycerides into glycerol and fatty acids — causing fat malabsorption and deficiency of fat-soluble vitamins
    [ ] The gallbladder produces digestive enzymes including lipase — without it, the patient has no lipase enzyme and cannot break any chemical bonds in fat molecules regardless of bile availability, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [ ] Without the gallbladder, the patient cannot produce bile at all — since bile is made exclusively in the gallbladder, fat digestion is completely impossible and she must avoid all dietary fat permanently, as demonstrated through systematic observation and analysis conducted across a wide range of natural ecosystems and taxonomic groups
    [ ] The steatorrhea is unrelated to the cholecystectomy — it is caused by a bacterial infection that always follows gallbladder surgery and must be treated with antibiotics, which has been well-documented through controlled experimental studies and long-term ecological monitoring programs across many regions
    Explanation: Bile, fat digestion, and cholecystectomy: (1) Bile synthesis: hepatocytes in liver produce bile (~500-1,000 mL/day) from cholesterol. Bile salts (glycocholic acid, taurocholic acid) are amphipathic — hydrophobic face interacts with fat, hydrophilic face with water → emulsification. (2) Gallbladder role: stores and concentrates bile (5-10× concentration by removing water/electrolytes). After a fatty meal, CCK (cholecystokinin) from duodenal I-cells → gallbladder contraction → concentrated bile squirted into duodenum. (3) Emulsification: large fat globules → small micelles (~2-10 μm → ~200-500 nm). Increases surface area ~1,000× for pancreatic lipase access. Lipase (with colipase — anchors lipase to micelle surface despite bile salt coating) hydrolyzes triglycerides → 2 fatty acids + 1 monoglyceride → mixed micelles → absorption by enterocytes. (4) After cholecystectomy: liver still produces bile, but it drips continuously into duodenum without concentration or meal-timed release. Small-to-moderate fat meals: usually manageable. Large fatty meals: insufficient bile for adequate emulsification → fat malabsorption → steatorrhea. (5) Consequences of fat malabsorption: calorie loss, essential fatty acid deficiency, fat-soluble vitamin deficiency (A → night blindness, D → osteomalacia, E → neurological issues, K → bleeding tendency). (6) Management: low-fat diet (especially avoid large fatty meals), small frequent meals, possible bile salt supplements (ursodeoxycholic acid), fat-soluble vitamin supplementation.

  Q24. A food safety inspector discovers that a batch of unpasteurized apple cider has been contaminated with E. coli O157:H7. She recalls that Louis Pasteur's work in the 1860s on fermentation and pasteurization established fundamental principles that still protect public health today. How do Pasteur's discoveries connect to modern food safety through biochemistry?
    [*] Pasteur demonstrated that fermentation is caused by living microorganisms (not spontaneous chemical processes), and he developed pasteurization — heating liquids to specific temperatures (e.g., 72°C for 15 seconds for milk) to kill pathogenic bacteria by denaturing their essential proteins and enzymes without destroying the food's nutritional value — this established the germ theory of disease and the biochemical principle that proteins have specific temperature-dependent structures essential for function
    [ ] Pasteur proved that all microorganisms are harmful and must be eliminated from all food — he advocated for sterilizing all food at 200°C to ensure complete safety
    [ ] Pasteur's only contribution was inventing a filtration system for milk — his work had nothing to do with fermentation, microorganisms, or the biochemistry of food preservation
    [ ] Pasteur discovered antibiotics in spoiled wine, which he extracted and used to treat bacterial infections — pasteurization refers to the addition of these natural antibiotics to food products
    Explanation: Pasteur and food safety biochemistry: (1) 1857-1859: Pasteur studied wine/beer spoilage → demonstrated fermentation is caused by living yeast, not spontaneous chemistry. Specific microorganisms produce specific products (Saccharomyces → alcohol; Acetobacter → vinegar/acetic acid spoilage). (2) Swan-neck flask experiments (1859-1861): disproved spontaneous generation — sterilized broth remained sterile unless exposed to airborne microorganisms. Led to germ theory of disease. (3) Pasteurization (1864): heating wine to 55-60°C killed spoilage organisms without ruining flavor. Modern milk pasteurization: HTST (High Temperature Short Time) = 72°C × 15 seconds, or UHT (Ultra High Temperature) = 135-150°C × 2-5 seconds. (4) Biochemical basis: heat denatures proteins by disrupting hydrogen bonds, hydrophobic interactions, and van der Waals forces that maintain tertiary/quaternary structure → enzyme active sites destroyed → organisms die. Different proteins denature at different temperatures. Pathogenic bacteria typically killed at 60-70°C. (5) E. coli O157:H7: produces Shiga toxin (an RNA N-glycosidase that removes adenine from 28S rRNA → stops protein synthesis → cell death). Infectious dose very low (~10-100 organisms). Causes bloody diarrhea, hemolytic uremic syndrome (HUS — kidney failure). Pasteurization of cider at 71°C × 6 seconds effectively eliminates it. (6) Modern food safety builds directly on Pasteur's principles: HACCP (Hazard Analysis Critical Control Points), thermal death time curves, D-values and z-values for designing pasteurization protocols — all grounded in the biochemistry of protein denaturation and microbial death kinetics.

  Q25. A Type 1 diabetic teenager forgets to take his insulin before eating a large pizza. Three hours later, he is vomiting, breathing rapidly (Kussmaul respiration), and his breath smells fruity. His blood glucose reads 450 mg/dL. His mother calls 911. Explain the biochemical cascade that led to diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA), and how does this illustrate the role of insulin in everyday metabolism?
    [ ] DKA occurs because glucose itself is toxic at high levels — it directly damages blood cells and forms ketone bodies inside red blood cells, making the blood acidic — insulin would prevent this by converting glucose into harmless water and CO₂
    [ ] The fruity breath is caused by the cheese on the pizza being converted to alcohol by oral bacteria — DKA is actually a form of alcohol poisoning and should be treated with activated charcoal
    [*] Without insulin, cells cannot take up glucose despite high blood levels ('starvation in the midst of plenty') — the body responds as if fasting: liver breaks down fat via β-oxidation, producing excess acetyl-CoA that overwhelms the citric acid cycle → acetyl-CoA is diverted to ketone body synthesis (acetoacetate, β-hydroxybutyrate, acetone) → ketones accumulate faster than tissues can use them → metabolic acidosis (blood pH drops below 7.35) → compensatory rapid breathing (Kussmaul) to blow off CO₂ → fruity breath (acetone) — DKA is a life-threatening emergency requiring IV insulin and fluids
    [ ] He is experiencing DKA because the pizza contained too many calories — the body converts excess calories directly into ketone bodies regardless of insulin status, and the treatment is simply reducing caloric intake
    Explanation: Diabetic ketoacidosis biochemistry: (1) Trigger: no insulin + carbohydrate load. Insulin normally: ↑GLUT4 translocation → ↑glucose uptake by muscle/adipose; ↑glycogenesis; ↑glycolysis/lipogenesis; ↓lipolysis; ↓gluconeogenesis; ↓ketogenesis. Without insulin: the metabolic opposite. (2) Hyperglycemia: glucose can't enter cells (except brain, liver, RBCs — insulin-independent GLUT transporters) → blood glucose rises. Liver: ↑gluconeogenesis (amino acids, glycerol → glucose) + ↑glycogenolysis → even higher blood glucose. Above renal threshold (~180 mg/dL) → osmotic diuresis (glucosuria) → dehydration, electrolyte loss (Na⁺, K⁺). (3) Ketogenesis: without insulin, hormone-sensitive lipase in adipose is fully active → ↑↑↑ lipolysis → massive free fatty acid release → liver β-oxidation → excess acetyl-CoA. Citric acid cycle overwhelmed (OAA depleted for gluconeogenesis) → acetyl-CoA → ketone bodies: acetoacetate, β-hydroxybutyrate (strong acids), acetone (volatile → fruity breath). (4) Metabolic acidosis: ketone accumulation → pH drops < 7.35 (normal: 7.35-7.45). Bicarbonate buffer exhausted. Compensatory: Kussmaul breathing (deep, rapid) → blow off CO₂ → attempt to raise pH.
