(5) Treatment: IV insulin (drives glucose into cells, stops ketogenesis), IV fluids (correct dehydration — average deficit 5-7L), electrolyte replacement (especially K⁺ — insulin drives K⁺ into cells → hypokalemia risk → cardiac arrhythmia). (6) Mortality: untreated DKA was uniformly fatal before insulin discovery (Banting & Best, 1921). With treatment, mortality ~1-5% (higher in developing countries).

  Q26. A winemaker in Napa Valley notices that one of her barrels of Cabernet Sauvignon has developed a sharp, vinegar-like taste. She tests it and finds high acetic acid levels. Meanwhile, her Champagne production relies on a secondary fermentation inside sealed bottles to produce carbonation. Explain the different biochemical processes occurring in each situation.
    [ ] The vinegar taste develops because ethanol spontaneously decomposes into acetic acid over time through a non-biological chemical reaction — no microorganisms are involved in either process
    [*] The spoiled wine involves Acetobacter bacteria performing aerobic oxidation of ethanol to acetic acid (ethanol + O₂ → acetaldehyde → acetic acid) — this requires oxygen entering through a faulty barrel seal — while Champagne's secondary fermentation involves Saccharomyces yeast performing anaerobic alcohol fermentation (glucose → ethanol + CO₂) in a sealed bottle, where the CO₂ cannot escape and dissolves into the wine under pressure, creating carbonation
    [ ] Both situations involve the same organism (Saccharomyces cerevisiae) performing the same type of fermentation — the different results are due solely to temperature differences between the barrel and the bottle
    [ ] Champagne gets its carbonation from injecting CO₂ gas into the bottles after fermentation is complete — the sealed bottle prevents the gas from escaping, but no biochemical reaction occurs inside the bottle
    Explanation: Wine biochemistry — fermentation vs. spoilage: (1) Primary wine fermentation: Saccharomyces cerevisiae (yeast) performs anaerobic alcohol fermentation: C₆H₁₂O₆ → 2 C₂H₅OH + 2 CO₂. Grape juice (~200 g/L sugar) → wine (~12-14% ethanol). Takes 1-3 weeks. CO₂ escapes through airlock. (2) Wine spoilage (acetification): Acetobacter aceti and related bacteria are obligate aerobes. If oxygen enters the barrel (bad seal, ullage not topped up): ethanol + O₂ → (alcohol dehydrogenase) → acetaldehyde → (aldehyde dehydrogenase) → acetic acid (vinegar). This is actually AEROBIC oxidation, not fermentation. Wine vinegar production deliberately exploits this process. Prevention: keep barrels full, minimize headspace, use SO₂ (sulfite) as antimicrobial/antioxidant. (3) Méthode champenoise (traditional method for Champagne and sparkling wines): after primary fermentation, wine is bottled with additional sugar + yeast → secondary fermentation in sealed bottle → CO₂ cannot escape → dissolves in wine under pressure (~6 atm/90 psi). Creates ~49 million bubbles per glass. After fermentation complete, bottles are riddled (rotated) and disgorged (frozen neck, remove yeast plug) → dosage (sugar + wine added to adjust sweetness). (4) Biochemical contrast: spoilage = aerobic oxidation (requires O₂, produces acid); Champagne = anaerobic fermentation (no O₂, produces ethanol + CO₂, CO₂ trapped → bubbles). Both are microbial metabolism but completely different pathways.

  Q27. A dermatologist explains to a patient with severe sunburn that UV-B radiation caused the formation of thymine dimers in the DNA of her skin cells, and that her repair enzymes are working to fix the damage. The patient asks why this matters. Using your knowledge of everyday biochemistry, explain how UV damage, DNA repair, and antioxidants relate to skin cancer risk.
    [ ] UV radiation has no direct effect on DNA — skin cancer is caused solely by melanin pigment accumulating in cells and triggering uncontrolled division, which is why darker-skinned individuals have higher skin cancer rates, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [ ] UV radiation only affects the outer dead skin cells (stratum corneum) and never reaches living cells with DNA — skin cancer is caused exclusively by chemical exposure, not sunlight, as governed by the molecular machinery of gene expression, protein synthesis, and intracellular transport within living cells overall
    [ ] Thymine dimers are actually beneficial — they help cells produce more vitamin D from sunlight, and DNA repair enzymes should be inhibited to maximize vitamin D production, through the action of specific biochemical pathways and regulatory mechanisms that maintain cellular homeostasis and normal growth
    [*] UV-B radiation causes adjacent thymine (or cytosine) bases on the same DNA strand to form covalent bonds, distorting the double helix and blocking replication/transcription — nucleotide excision repair (NER) normally removes and replaces damaged segments, but if repair fails or is overwhelmed by excessive UV exposure, mutations accumulate — these mutations in tumor suppressor genes (e.g., p53) or proto-oncogenes can initiate skin cancer — sunscreen blocks UV
    Explanation: UV damage and skin cancer biochemistry: (1) UV-B (280-315 nm) directly absorbed by DNA bases → adjacent pyrimidines form covalent bonds: cyclobutane pyrimidine dimers (CPDs, most common: T=T, T=C) and 6-4 photoproducts. (2) Repair: nucleotide excision repair (NER) — a multi-enzyme system that recognizes bulky DNA distortions, excises ~24-32 nucleotides around the damage, and resynthesizes using the undamaged strand as template. In humans, ~10 proteins involved (XPA through XPG). Xeroderma pigmentosum (XP): autosomal recessive mutations in NER genes → inability to repair UV damage → 1,000× increased skin cancer risk → patients must avoid all sunlight. (3) Mutation signature: if unrepaired CPD is replicated → DNA polymerase inserts adenine opposite the dimer → C→T or CC→TT transitions. p53 tumor suppressor gene frequently mutated in skin cancers — specific 'UV signature' mutations confirm UV as causative. (4) UV-A (315-400 nm, penetrates deeper) → primarily indirect damage: generates ROS (singlet oxygen, superoxide) → oxidative DNA damage (8-oxoguanine), lipid peroxidation, protein oxidation. Antioxidants (vitamin C, E, polyphenols from green tea/berries) can neutralize these ROS. (5) Skin cancer statistics: most common cancer globally. Basal cell carcinoma (most common, rarely metastatic), squamous cell carcinoma, melanoma (deadliest — ~8,000 deaths/year in US). UV exposure is primary modifiable risk factor. (6) Sunscreen biochemistry: mineral/physical sunscreens (zinc oxide, titanium dioxide) reflect UV; chemical sunscreens (oxybenzone, avobenzone) absorb UV and dissipate as heat.

  Q28. A biotechnology company uses genetically engineered bacteria to produce human insulin for diabetic patients. The bacteria carry a plasmid containing the human insulin gene, and the insulin is purified from bacterial culture medium. A new employee asks how bacteria can produce a human protein. Explain the biochemical basis for recombinant protein production.
    [ ] Bacteria produce human insulin by absorbing the human insulin protein from the culture medium and modifying it with their own enzymes — no gene transfer is involved
    [ ] The bacteria don't produce insulin — they simply grow around insulin crystals added to the medium and are used as a packaging material for drug delivery
    [ ] Bacteria cannot actually produce human proteins — the 'recombinant insulin' is actually a synthetic chemical that mimics insulin's shape but has a completely different amino acid sequence
    [*] The genetic code is nearly universal — the same codons specify the same amino acids in bacteria and humans — so when the human insulin gene (DNA) is inserted into a bacterial plasmid, the bacterial RNA polymerase transcribes it into mRNA, and bacterial ribosomes translate the mRNA into human insulin protein with the correct amino acid sequence — this works because the central dogma (DNA → RNA → Protein) and the genetic code are shared across all life
    Explanation: Recombinant insulin production — biochemistry meets biotechnology: (1) Historical context: before 1982, diabetic patients used insulin purified from pigs or cows (slightly different amino acid sequences → allergic reactions in some patients). Genentech/Eli Lilly produced first recombinant human insulin (Humulin) in 1982 — first FDA-approved recombinant DNA drug. (2) Why it works: the genetic code is (nearly) universal — AUG = methionine, UUU = phenylalanine, etc., in both humans and E. coli. A codon-optimized human insulin gene in E. coli will produce the same amino acid sequence as human pancreatic β-cells. (3) Process: human insulin gene (or synthetic DNA based on known insulin amino acid sequence) → insert into expression vector (plasmid with strong promoter, e.g., lac promoter + IPTG inducer, antibiotic resistance marker, origin of replication) → transform into E. coli → grow bacteria in fermenters → induce expression → harvest cells → lyse → purify insulin (chromatography: ion exchange, HPLC, size exclusion). (4) Insulin structure: initially produced as preproinsulin (signal peptide + B chain + C peptide + A chain) → processed: signal peptide cleaved, C peptide removed, A and B chains linked by 2 disulfide bonds (A7-B7, A20-B19) + internal disulfide (A6-A11). In bacteria, often produce A and B chains separately → refold and oxidize in vitro. (5) Modern variants: insulin analogs engineered by changing amino acids — rapid-acting (lispro: swap Pro-Lys at B28-B29 → prevents hexamer formation → faster absorption), long-acting (glargine: Asn→Gly at A21 + 2 Arg at B31-B32 → forms microprecipitates at injection site → slow release over 24h). (6) Scale: ~100 million diabetics worldwide use insulin. Global insulin market ~$30 billion/year.

  Q29. A food scientist is developing a new sports drink and must decide between using sucrose (table sugar), glucose, fructose, or a combination. She knows that different sugars are absorbed at different rates through different intestinal transporters. Athletes need rapid energy during competition. Which biochemical factors should she consider, and why do most modern sports drinks use a mixture of sugars?
    [ ] Fructose is the only sugar that can be used directly by muscles during exercise — glucose and sucrose must first be converted to fructose in the liver before muscles can use them, making fructose-only drinks the best choice
    [ ] All sugars are absorbed identically through the same transporter — the choice doesn't matter biochemically, and sports drinks use mixtures solely for marketing to appeal to broader taste preferences
    [*] Different sugars use different intestinal transporters — glucose is absorbed via SGLT1 (sodium-glucose linked transporter, which becomes saturated at ~60g/hour) and exits via GLUT2, while fructose uses GLUT5 (a separate, independent transporter) — mixing glucose and fructose allows simultaneous absorption through both pathways, increasing total carbohydrate delivery to ~90g/hour (vs. ~60g from glucose alone), providing faster energy delivery and causing less gastrointestinal distress than a single sugar at high concentration (lower osmolarity)
    [ ] Sports drinks should contain only proteins because muscles need amino acids, not sugars, during exercise — sugars are stored for post-exercise recovery only
    Explanation: Sports nutrition biochemistry: (1) Sugar absorption: small intestine has multiple transporters with different capacities. SGLT1 (sodium-glucose cotransporter 1): absorbs glucose + galactose from intestinal lumen → enterocyte using Na⁺ gradient (active transport). Rate-limited at ~1g/min or ~60g/hour. GLUT5: absorbs fructose by facilitated diffusion (independent pathway, lower capacity). GLUT2 (basolateral): exports all monosaccharides into blood. (2) Multiple transportable carbohydrates (MTC) strategy: glucose + fructose together utilize SGLT1 + GLUT5 simultaneously → total absorption up to ~90g/hour (Jentjens et al., 2004). Pure glucose alone maxes out at ~60g/hour → excess causes GI distress (osmotic diarrhea). (3) Sucrose = glucose + fructose (α-1,2 glycosidic bond) → hydrolyzed by brush border sucrase → provides both sugars. Many sports drinks use glucose polymers (maltodextrin — chains of glucose with α-1,4 bonds) + fructose: maltodextrin has low osmolarity per unit glucose (one long molecule vs. many free glucose molecules → less osmotic pull → less GI distress) but still absorbed as glucose after pancreatic amylase/maltase digestion. (4) Optimal sports drink: 6-8% carbohydrate concentration (~60-80g/L), sodium (enhances SGLT1 transport and fluid absorption), 2:1 glucose-to-fructose ratio. (5) Elite endurance athletes (Tour de France, Ironman) now target 90-120g carbohydrate/hour using trained gut protocols with MTC drinks. (6) Glycemic index consideration: during exercise, high GI is desired (rapid absorption) — opposite of general health advice (low GI for blood sugar stability).

  Q30. A scientist discovers a new extremophilic bacterium living in volcanic hot springs at 95°C. She isolates an enzyme from this organism that digests proteins (a protease) and finds it works optimally at 90°C — it barely functions at 37°C (human body temperature). A pharmaceutical company wants to use this enzyme in a medical application that requires activity at 37°C. How does the relationship between enzyme structure and temperature explain this problem, and what approaches might solve it?
    [ ] The enzyme doesn't work at 37°C because it has completely denatured at that low temperature — enzymes always denature below their optimal temperature, losing all structure permanently, involving the coordinated activity of organelles, enzymes, and signaling pathways that regulate cellular function and reproduction
    [ ] The problem is unsolvable — an enzyme from a thermophile can never be modified to work at lower temperatures because the DNA encoding it would become unstable at lower temperatures and stop producing the protein, as governed by the molecular machinery of gene expression, protein synthesis, and intracellular transport within living cells overall
    [ ] The enzyme only works at 90°C because it requires water vapor (steam) as a substrate — at 37°C, water is liquid and cannot enter the active site in the correct form, through the action of specific biochemical pathways and regulatory mechanisms that maintain cellular homeostasis and normal growth
    [*] The thermophilic enzyme has evolved extra stabilizing features that make it extremely stable but also very rigid at lower temperatures — at 37°C, the enzyme is too rigid for the conformational flexibility needed for catalysis — solutions include protein engineering, chemical modification, or creating chimeric enzymes with elements from mesophilic homologs
    Explanation: Enzyme structure-function and temperature: (1) Thermophilic adaptations: enzymes from organisms like Thermus aquaticus (source of Taq polymerase, optimal at 72°C) or Pyrococcus furiosus (optimal ~100°C) have evolved structural features for stability at extreme temperatures: increased ionic interactions (salt bridges: Arg-Glu pairs), more disulfide bonds (Cys-Cys), compact hydrophobic cores (tighter packing → less cavity space), reduced loop regions, higher proline content (restricts backbone flexibility), decreased glycine content (glycine = most flexible amino acid), increased hydrogen bonds. (2) Activity-stability trade-off: the same features that confer thermal stability (rigidity) reduce catalytic activity at lower temperatures. Enzymes require conformational flexibility for substrate binding (induced fit) and catalysis. At 37°C, a thermophilic enzyme is 'frozen' in its conformation — kcat drops dramatically. (3) Solutions: Directed evolution (Frances Arnold, Nobel 2018): create library of random mutations → screen for variants with activity at 37°C while maintaining stability → iterate. Rational design: use structural information to introduce specific mutations (e.g., Gly→Pro to add flexibility at specific sites near active site). Site-directed mutagenesis: replace stabilizing features (remove salt bridges, reduce disulfide bonds) in a targeted way. Domain swapping/chimeras: combine thermostable structural regions with catalytically active mesophilic domains. (4) Practical examples: Taq polymerase (optimal 72°C) is used in PCR specifically because it survives the 95°C denaturation step. Cold-adapted enzymes from psychrophilic organisms (Antarctic fish, deep-sea bacteria) are the opposite — very flexible, active at 0-10°C, but denature at 30-40°C. Used in sensitive detergents for cold-water washing, food processing.
